RATING: 8
Heavy yet cutely themed game, everything ties in together nicely. Seems to work decently at all player counts, the moving blockers in the 2p game aren't too much hassle. Initially feels rather random with the needs cards but much of this can be mitigated with good play. Feels fairly straightforward after half a dozen plays; you know what you need to do and you have to play the players to figure out how best to do it. So the worker placement system retains some freshness. I think Lords retains its 'meatiness' better though.

Both of Vlaada's Dungeon X games bring ways for players to think/act simultaneously in a worker placement game, which I think is wonderful.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A worker placement game (yes, again), where you use your imps (workers) to procure baby monsters and other materials in order to raise and "show off" and sell the monsters, both of which score you the points in the game.

This game has several good things going for it, despite the fact that it enters an extremely crowded genre: great artwork and components, scales well from 2-4 players, funny theme which works well and leads to intuitive gameplay (well mostly), and a somewhat unique selection mechanic (grouping of imps).  It's odd in that it's a reasonably complex stragey game, but also it has fairly significant random elements (card draws, customers), but these elements are very manageable and the theme is "fun" enough that it's not a big issue when things don't fall your way.  I mean I spent one game frantically shovelling poo out of my cages, and had times when I was picking up meat only to have it rot before I could use it, but that was more funny than frustrating.  It was also enjoyable to just edge out my competition (multiple times) by whipping my monster into "best in show".  

Gameplay is quite accessible, and overall this is a much better, and different, game than it's predecessor, Dungeon Lords.  

Own the [boardgame=143061]Dark Alleys[/boardgame] expansion, and the [boardgame=151254]Bob the Blob[/boardgame] and [boardgame=126247]Bonus Pets[/boardgame] promos.

RATING: 8
Thematic, entertaining, and a very serious strategy game despite the playful theme. It can be very challenging to teach this game, due to the simultaneous action selection, worker placement, and most importantly the meetings needs mechanic. I love the theme, but I do wish the game had a less playful title as it ends up dissuading more serious players, even though this game is a solid medium-heavy worker placement game with rather unique mechanics. What I love about this game: it compels you to tell stories of your pets, you remember your experience playing it, there is always a feeling of scarcity, and the game is beautifully produced with amazing details where they're meaningful (for example, each minion sticker is unique, each player board is unique, adorable pet art, the imps are perfect).

This one can join RftG as objectively good games that I suck at!

RATING: 7.5
I like Dungeon Petz, mostly. It's a bit too long, a bit too random, a bit too overcomplicated -- all the "too"s we expect from Vlaada sometimes. But it's also rather fun, and while I don't see it being that much "lighter" than the awful Dungeon Lords, it spends the time and depth on your actions, not on game-driven upkeep and automatic happenings. The psuedo-auction can be annoying, but it's more interesting than the constraints of Dungeon Lords. I'll probably play this occasionally, and rather like it. The manual's a blast.

RATING: 6
2-4(4)

It's fine enough, really cute theme and some interesting mechanisms. The blind bidding has some interesting decision-making and press-your-luck (in both deciding how big to make your groups and how many to commit and how many to keep for tending your animals).

It's pretty fun but I'm not sure how much here is new. It's a touch tense, like Agricola but, and I'm not sure if we maybe played wrong, it was pretty easy to avoid suffering markers. Maybe we just focused too much on tending animals (as opposed to means of scoring). The dummy player was pretty simple and it all felt like it worked with two.

I've been a detractor of Vlaada's rules before, but I thought these rules were actually pretty effective and funny (not necessary but certain nice).

RATING: 7
I had heard that Dungeon Petz incorporated some fun and novel worker placement mechanics and was thematically VERY strong...and found this to be quite true.

Interesting game, not exactly an accessible game to the general public, or easily taught even to gamers, since the rules set is practically advanced war game length.

The emphasis in Dungeon Petz is so clearly on more and more details, and NOT on making the game elegant in its simplicity or ease to play, it is laughable. This game wants to include everything possibly pertinent to the theme.

The theme is...dare I say it...cute...and ironically, considering the fact that pet ownership in western culture is a huge part of millions of people's lives...should be VERY resonant with millions of "potential gamers." In other words, pet "ownership and upkeep" games in theory could have a potentially HUGE audience.

Dungeon Petz, however, is not the game that is going to reach that potentially huge audience, at least not until they enter into "pet ownership and upkeep gaming" through a different gateway game...yet to be designed, then want something much more "advanced" and reach for Dungeon Petz.

Dungeon Petz definitely meets the definition of a board game as being "art" and "a distinctive world that you enter." Well done.

RATING: 7
Fantastic artwork and theme. All of the actions fit together very nicely and help sell the entire theme of the game. Well written rules are very entertaining but can be difficult to remember. Setup time at the start of the game and between turns is very time consuming and becomes tiring as it drags down the already slow pace of the game. There are a number of obscure things in this game that can be difficult to remember that really irritate my wife that keeps her from really enjoying this one. Between the hard to remember rules, fiddly setup, and the length vs. enjoyment factor of this game, we can't really see ourselves playing this again; there are too many other better games that fit into this time frame that are less annoying. Sold to make room in the collection.

RATING: 9.2
The 'sequel' to Dungeon Lords is just as vivid and animated, with distinct artwork and an even more unique theme. Raise, exhibit and sell a variety of zany monster pets, but you'll need to meet their needs. Smooth worker placement mechanics, colorful components, all part of a surprisingly deep game with a lot of decisions to be made. A bit more involved than your typical Medium Euro, but well worth learning, and the rulebook is so hilarious that I must have caught myself laughing out loud a half dozen times, and it just makes the learning all the more engaging.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.1
I love how the game's mechanics make the pets both assets and liabilities (just like a baby pet Cthulu should be!)  You want your pet to be angry enough to impress a customer, but not so angry that it breaks its cage.  Hungry enough to win the eating contest, but only when you have enough food to feed it.  Having the pets' needs be somewhat probabilistic (though not completely) via the need cards was a great design choice, and it really brings the pets to life.  This system also adds a push-your-luck component to the game: should I splurge on the marketplace this round, or should I keep my imp at home, just in case Snakitty is feeling extra playful today?

Also, this game has lots of poop, and poop is funny!

Cons for this game include the complexity of its rules.  It might be the hardest game to learn in my collection... although the rules are easy to remember due to their thematic consistency. It's also fairly fiddly.  At the beginning of every round, restocking the marketplace (cages, addons, food, artifacts, pets) is a bit annoying, especially if you're also taking the dummy imps into account when playing with less than 4 players.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Azonos pontot adok rá, mint a Lordsra, nehezen tudnám őket rangsorolni. Van amiben egyik jobb, van amiben a másik. Sok a megegyező elem, hasonló a téma, hasonló a stílus. De a Petz vidámabb, barátságosabb hangulatú. A Lordsnál bonyolultabb egy kör menete, a Petznél ez kicsit egysíkúbb: állatokat veszünk, nevelünk, majd eladunk... 

A legnagyobb különbség az akcióválasztásban van: a Lords mechanizmusa is nagyon érdekes, a Petznek is ez az erőssége szerintem: amiben eltér a hagyományos worker placementtől, hogy itt több munkás is mehet egy helyre, titkosan állítjuk be, hogy hány munkásból álló csapatokat küldenénk ki, és ezek létszáma határozza meg a sorrendet. Utána a szokásos akcióválasztás történik.

Ezen kívül is további nagyon sok érdekes ötlet van: az állatok fejlődése profin van megoldva a korongokon, jók a távoli rokonok, a szar a ketrecben, a gombolyag, amivel játszanak az állatok:)... láthatóan a szerző bármilyen témából képes összegyúrni egy jól működő soktényezős minden-mindennel összefügg játékot.
És szokás szerint vicces is a játék (soha egy játék alatt sem hangzott el ennyiszer a "szar" szó.:))

Ami nem annyira tetszett, az a kártyák miatti szerencsefaktor. A végső eladásoknál brutális különbségek adódhattak ebből. Ehhez képest nekem túl hosszú a játék (1 óra szabály, 3 óra játék). Órákig szerezgetjük a pontokat a körvégi versenyekből, eladásokból, és a végén pár jó kártyával akár megduplázhatjuk addigi pontjainkat.

Szerintem nem könnyű játék, de jó szórakozás.

Szép, vicces, összességében örülök, hogy kipróbáltam, de nagy kedvenc nem lesz azért, bár szerethetőbb, mint a Lords...

RATING: 9
Ok. This is a good game. It reminds me of Drum roll, another economic game in which the financial aspect is hidden behind the richness of the theme. Actually these 2 games have more in common than meets the eye. The different abstract-coloured cubes/needs of the performers/animals is another similarity.

The best part in Petz is explaining the rules! after that, the first play is a bit introductory. On your second play you begin to realize the economic engine a bit better. It is a good game but not one that will have you jumping up and down from excitement. The choices are balanced and the theme is very rich. It is a perfectly fine semi-economic euro with many small innovations. The rules actually sound a lot harder than the gameplay really is. Its complexity is the same as Dungeon Lords, and its difficulty is lighter.

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Vlaada strikes again: 2011 is a great vintage for the czech designer if this is his second most important game of that year (number one is of course Mage Knight)!
Dungeon Petz is a worker placement game merged with some blind bidding: every player secretly groups his imps, and lager groups will be placed earlier. This leads to a fierce competition for most useful placements, and to very tense games.
The game is thematically great, with a fantastic rulebook, awesome artwork and a great graphic design (I love the functional petz, with their "egg" backside), and its probably better that its predecessor Dungeon Lords (they share some fiddlyness, but Dungeon Petz is probably less fiddly).
Last but not least: it's a great 2-player game (the dummy player is fast to implement and the movement of its imps can be taken into account when planning)!
Can't wait to try it again, even with the advanced variant...

Our first Vlaada Chvatil game.

RATING: 5.5
First Play: The rules take a while to absorb and a while to explain but the rule book is logical and if you have played Dungeon Lords, it has a similar flow.

After one play the jury is out. Yes the build quality is excellent and there is so much to think about. Mistakes are dealt with brutally and when your petz start to suffer they are almost impossible to sell. The incident pit deepens and you know early on that you wont win. I don't think that is a bad thing, quite the opposite, however my reservations centre on the 'Need' Cards. 

Under tha basic rules the cards have one value and although there is a frequency based upon colour the deal is utterly random. A bad deal can cut you off at the ankles or set you up with a sale worth over 20 points. This disparity is eased slightly by the advanced variant that offers a second choice to some cards, but the random nature of the draw still remains.

Some players may like this randomness, I need a second play but I fear that this issue may result in me selling the game on swiftly if there isn't a course of action to mitigate this chaos.

Second Play: Yes I managed to build a suitable foundation to support varying needs but I could do nothing to influence the cards for exhibitions and sales. This is what I now believe to tip the game towards being more heavily influenced by luck than applied strategy. A fun game, but not one I wish to keep in my collection as I would rather play Dungeon Lords any time over this.

Sold 14th Nov 2011

Persuaded to play to demo for a friend. By now this is quite a joyless experience. The fun of jostling for positions on the board is so fatally spoiled by the anticipation of what need cards will be drawn. Good and bad draws are treated with a fatalistic shrug, success and failure are mainly results out of your control.

RATING: 2
Initial impressions:

Absolutely terrible, waste-of-time game.  Well, the base game is.

The game is almost reasonable enough, with the massive exception of receiving all  your card "income" at the end of the turn when you can change nothing with the random cards you receive.  Oh look, all my reds are play cards instead of at least one happy card...guess I lose.  Complete waste of time, why not play two-card Candyland (you receive two cards and "choose" between them - hey look, meaningful "decisions!").

So...the idea is to modify it, to change the timing of your income to the beginning of the turns, or when you grab a new income source.  Also, using the "advanced/better" rules of having all the buyers/competitions face-up at the beginning, as well as using the advanced cards.

With those improvements, this is probably a 4 or a 5 - a reasonably light game still overburdened with too much superficial complexity for what the game is worth.  The only real interesting decisions here are what your imp groups will look like, and when to sell your animals over the course of the game.  The rest is just optimization.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game absolutely oozes theme and personality while maintaining a highly competitive game space with deeply rich decisions. Player interaction comes through during blind bidding of shopping groups for worker placement, where you have to weigh out essentially having more actions vs having earlier actions and taking things before your opponents. It's tight and tense and wonderful! There are a lot of things to do, but a very limited number of spaces. And then we have the actual caring for the petz themselves, which allows a high level of strategy while necessitating tactical flexibility because it really does feel like you are trying to meet the needs of a living thing with somewhat unpredictable wants. So you have to decide how much you want to press your luck at every turn, both through blind bidding for actions and preparing for the unexpected with pet care. It's incredibly competitive and complex without ever being dense due to the amazingly intuitive nature of the rules. Everything just makes sense. And it's hilarious! The rules made me laugh out loud. I cannot stress enough how much personality is packed into this one, enhanced by the gorgeous artwork. If I were to say one minor drawback, it's that luck can occasionally be too great a factor. This is generally rare, however, and the more skilled play wins out more often than not. I do think it plays best with the full player count and loses a tiny bit of it's greatness when scaled down to lower player counts.

The thematic integration is excellent for a worker placement Euro.  The well-integrated theme also makes understanding the game much easier than other Euros of a similar weight (IMHO).  The rule book is entertaining, the gameplay tight and smooth, and the length just about right (once learned).  

And, most importantly, my wife loves it so regardless of game group availability, it actually gets to the table.

HOWEVER, it is also fiddly and has a lot of randomness (primarily the card draw) that is difficult to mitigate and surprising in what is primarily a strategic game.

RATING: 6
Right now, this one is a little underwhelming.  I like the theme and the art, but in all honesty, I find it a little limp.  I'm not sure why.  The lack of tension comes from how subtle the game actually is.  Are you going to frequently be unable to meet your animals' needs?  Likely not.  When you do have to let one of your pets down, it will likely involve a small, incremental amount of badness.  Likely the animals will suffer a little by the end of the game, but you'd have to mess up pretty bad to have them really hurting.  Thus, the monthly competitions feel a little underwhelming: "My critter has one less poop than yours, so I score 4 and you score 3.  Ha!"  The game just doesn't feel as hectic and crazy as you expect it to.  Is there a good Euro in here, though?  Well, it doesn't help that the first 3 times we played, we got something wrong (a "worst-ever" for us in this regard).  So - I am unsure.  But my got-it-right 3 player game was not significantly more exciting.  So, right now I'd say it's decent - maybe I'll like it more with more plays, or maybe it will hit the trade pile.  We'll see.

RATING: 7
Dungeon Lords was interesting but more than a bit on the long and footery side and I was looking forward to this as the early feedback seemed to suggest it had improved on those things. [b]TOTAL = 367.5/50 = 7.35[/b]


:thumbsup: [b]POSITIVE POINTS:[/b]

++ A very thematic strategy eurogame, half  simultaneous action selection (at least, a kind of basic worker placement plan for the round), half worker placement, where mechanisms and theme integrate really well.
++ The theme is really appealing and original and the art is cute and funny.
++ I really like the partly predictable pets needs, because you'll have to be creative to workaround this randomness to then pursue your strategy.
++ I like that it's a game with no direct confrontation that play well at 2-players (I don't mind the dummy imps).
++ There is an expansion available for this game (which is useful to refresh the game when we have overplayed it). 


:thumbsdown: [b]NEGATIVE POINTS:[/b]

-- There are many rules that, even though not complicated, must be assimilated correctly.
-- There is a lot of steps in each phase of each round and a lot of upkeep (this is not a streamlined elegant Euro like the one I love), so the game flow is always halted.
-- This game is way longer than I like (although to be fair, time pass fairly quickly while playing, as there is not a lot of downtime because some parts of the game can be played simultaneously) but I will probably never play this games with more than 2 players.
-- There doesn't seem to have anything new or innovative in this game.
-- The game has so many components and boards that it take a lot of table space.
-- The rule book, although funny to read, is not really practical and is hard to find the needed information.
-- This is a heavy game (despite the fun/cute theme) and it's at the limit of being too heavy / brainburner for my wife.
-- Harder and longer to teach to new people, because of all the rules, and also because you have to closely monitor new players to be sure they do everything correctly during the parts that are played simultaneously.


:arrowE: [b]OTHER GAME INFO:[/b]

2-player length = 120 min.
Player Count Preference = Best at 2, recommended at 3 and 4! :thumbsup::thumbsup:
[/b]

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Another cool twist on the worker placement genre, where players are tending to the needs of wacky pets, attempting to raise them for profit.  You'll find yourself tending to your pets' needs, while trying to cater to the fairly specific whims of buyers that come along to purchase your pets.  Each turn a buyer comes along that wants certain pet features; some buyers want a playful pet, some want an aggressive pet, and some even don't mind a pet that has been mistreated and is sitting around in its own feces.  Yeah, buyers are weird like that.

One interesting mechanism is the way worker placement is handled.  Players secretly create groups of imps (workers) behind their screens each turn.  When the groups are revealed, whoever placed the biggest group goes first, and play continues according to who currently has the largest group remaining until all groups are placed.  So, playing a couple large groups means you'll go first, but not get many actions, while playing several smaller groups means you'll get a lot of actions, but the choice actions might be taken by the time you get to place anything.  A pretty neat system.

Overall, this is a pretty cool little worker placement/resource management game where the theme really helps out the fun factor!

RATING: 10
Terrific art and sense of humor. Thematically solid. I love the way blind bidding is incorporated into the worker placement. Lovely components. It can take a while to explain everything but the rules are intuitive and the mechanics make perfect thematic sense (sending your imps out to bribe judges, bring home cages, buy pets, recover workers from the hospital or immigration office, etc.), so after a round or two everyone will know what they are doing and the game moves very quickly. Meeting the random needs of your pets while still having all the specific things to score in exhibitions and sell pets makes for a challenging yet fun puzzle element.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
2 plays in:
The "full rules" are unsurprisingly much better. To Vlaada's credit, and also unsurprisingly, this feels like no other game. Well, the worker placement part does, but there's this thing where you get a bunch of cards representing "needs" of your pets (eating, poopoing, magic, anger, play) and you have to satisfy them through various means. Thematically your pets are autonomous, and you have to just deal with what they do. But gameplay-wise you get a big pool of needs and you assign them to different pets, which makes no sense. But makes for an interesting puzzle. And puzzle it is. Ultimately, I'm finding that these "puzzle" games (see Mage Knight) are fun, but not exactly my thing so much. Every turn feels like a puzzle to solve. But the good news is that everyone is solving it at the same time! That makes the game pretty accessible. But the puzzles tend to get more complicated as you get more pets, and not everyone has the same number of pets, so it can happen that some players' puzzles are significantly more complicated than others, which means some players can be waiting. Not a huge deal though. It has an element of blind bidding, which will not be everyone's cup of tea. I find that part to be a bit nerve-wracking, as I can never figure out what the right amount is. Rather than bid for one thing at a time, you're bidding for turn order of multiple actions at once.

RATING: 8
[b]Miniature Market - Easter 2015[/b] 
Very fun/whimsical game.  It is drenched in theme that appeases me.  I love the notion of raising monster petz and making sure they are happy...And they poo!  [microbadge=22882]  The way the imps are utilized as a auctioning turn order and worker placement works great.  Really makes you plan your turns in advance.  

There is a lot of planning that needs to be done in this game.  We have the goals of the customers shopping for petz, the display requirements, and the cards gathered/distributed during the rounds going towards the petz that need to be considered.  It [i]could[/i] lead to AP, but it's still a very fun game. 

I'd like to see this game come to the table more often, but recently I've been playing solitary two-player games.  This game feels rather long when I do it this way.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
A charming and thematically rich game of risk management and board reading, with a few interesting tweaks to classic worker placement design. Given the theme and artwork, new players are often unprepared for how complex it can be and the game can be pretty punishing for the first few plays; its saving grace is that it (mostly) manages to be fun even if you're losing quite badly. It's best to make new players' expectations clear ahead of time. I can't help but feel like certain elements could have been streamlined (the exhibition and sale phases always seem a little sluggish and overwrought) but the overall experience is still incredibly satisfying, frequently hilarious, and challenging in consistently interesting ways.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.2
Not nearly as complex or "indirect" as Dungeon Lords.  Your goals are clear and concise.  You raise monsters to show in exhibitions and to sell. 

This game is also much more "reactionary" as in you move into each round with a goal in mind and then you need to adjust to the randomness of the round.  But you have quite a lot of flexibility in what you can do to adjust to the randomness, so the player has quite alot of control of the events.  IMO, this makes the game much more "euro game like" than Dungeon Lords.

Great game.  Lots of fun.

RATING: 6
A lot is going on in this game, but the gist of it is that you plan your strategy and then hope to draw the cards that permit you to implement it --- the reverse of the more customary mechanism. All players have a chart that shows the card distributions, so you have an idea which cards are likely to appear. This mechanism probably would inject just the right amount of uncertainty into a simple and short game but does not work so well in this complex and fiddly game.

RATING: 7
I am fairly hesitant to rate Dungeon Petz because I am uncertain as to what my final opinion of it will be. I played it once at BGG.Con and quite enjoyed it, but it failed to really excite me like some of the other designs at the show did. Functionally it is another in the “Lets Put On A Show!” genre, where players prepare a presentation just in time for the big show to score victory points (other noteworthy games in the genre include Drum Roll, Pret-a-Porter, and Vinhos). As far the genre goes it is pretty good, on the same general level as Vinhos and a bit better than either Drum Roll or Pret-a-Porter. The theme is cute and the mechanics are pretty interesting. The worker placement/blind bidding hybrid is rather unique and appeals to me despite my normal aversion for blind bidding and the hand management/set collection involved in the generation and meeting of needs also results in plenty of tensely interesting decisions. I don’t see any flaws per se in the design and I expect that even though it lacks the ability to hold my attention like Agricola, Caylus, or Ora et Labora it will still sit as a solid second-tier worker placement game that warrants occasional play if not obsessive attention.

RATING: 5
[b]Tipo:[/b] Competitivo // [b]Interacción:[/b] Media // [b]Azar:[/b] Medio // [b]Rejugabilidad:[/b] Media
[b]Cartas:[/b] 109 de 40x67 mm
[b]Fundas recomendadas:[/b] Ultimate Guard "Supreme" (tamaño "Mini european") [i](son la opción menos mala, pero no se adaptan muy bien)[/i]



[b]OPINIÓN PERSONAL:[/b]
Está claro que Dungeon Petz no es para nada un mal juego (de hecho diría que está bastante bien), pero tiene dos inconvenientes que lo hacen totalmente incompatible con mi grupo de juego:

 -> Es complejo. Puede que cada elemento por separado sea más o menos fácil/intuitivo, pero Dungeon Petz tiene muchas fases y subfases, y tienes que recordar cómo se llevan a cabo cada una de ellas, las opciones que tienes disponibles y para qué sirve cada elemento y zona de los múltiples tableros: en conjunto, es uno de esos juegos en los que te pasas las primeras dos o tres partidas jugando "a trompicones" (con continuas paradas para preguntar/consultar el manual para ver cómo se hacía determinada cosa) y en los que resulta bastante difícil ver la situación actual de la partida (por lo que resulta más complicado elaborar estrategias).

 -> El tema. Está claro que lo más importante es la jugabilidad, pero no se puede (o al menos yo no puedo) separar por completo un juego de su temática... y en este caso, esta lo lastra enormemente. Ya las ilustraciones son tirando a infantiles (hay quien las llamaría simplemente "monas"), pero es que el tema se lleva la palma: cuando les empecé a explicar a los de mi grupo de juego que no solo había que alimentar a las mascotas, sino también jugar con ellas y limpiar sus caquitas... en fin, digamos que estamos acostumbrados a jugar a cosas más "serias" (supongo que para un eurogame esperamos más las típicas temáticas de comercio y/o agricultura).

Tengo claro que el fracaso de este juego es (en gran parte) culpa de mi grupo de juego, y por eso no le pongo demasiada mala nota. Desde luego se lo recomiendo a todos aquellos que estén acostumbrados a juegos complejos y que no les importe lo más mínimo la temática.

RATING: 8
Long and procedural, but completely engaging the entire time.  The grouping of workers is a good concept, and so is the way cards are used to attempt to meet the needs of pets (and net you big points).   I also liked how you need to consider holding back some imps to play with the pets, restrain them, or clean cages.  There isn't a ton of long-range planning or large development arcs (as in Agricola).  Here you are mostly going for what will currently score well with a little foresight to the future.   The lack of downtime is a huge plus, given the relative length of the game (2-2.5 hrs).  The production is a bit cheap - the bits are small, the boards and pets are quite thin and flimsy.  I expect better for $65 retail.  Overall, a very enjoyable and unique middle-weight worker placement game.  Good stuff.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Vlaada Chvátil is among my favorite designers because he is always trying something new.  To say that Chvátil is a quirky designer understates some of his more genius strokes, and while he doesn't always hit the mark, Dungeon Petz is one of those strokes.

At its heart, Petz is an economic game driven by bidding and worker placement coupled with a great sense of humor that requires players to say "poop" hundreds of times in a single session.  The mechanics integrate well with the theme, but the pure joy of the game comes from the inevitable discussions that occur at the table.  This is not a game where there is heavy player interaction outside of the bidding mechanism, but ultimately, you will get more out of it if you chat at the table.

Such as it is, the game itself is almost perfectly limited in every aspect.  That is to say, at no time could you possibly do everything you want to do; not only are you competing for spaces on the board to buy cages, pets, and extremely valuable food for your creatures, but you'll also be looking at the upcoming customers, the exhibition requirements, and those magical boosts that people often ignore.  While new players often want to get as many pets as possible, like any economic game, the best strategy is to specialize with a pet or two and work towards the upcoming exhibitions and customers while everyone else is distracted by the funny looking polka-dotted rhino thing.  For example, why bother getting the really angry critter with red cards when the buyer wants a really hungry one that poops a lot?  With so much on-board competition going and very limited resources available, there's a lot of great choices to make with every turn and the down-time is minimal.

This is absolutely a game everyone needs to play, and I will always be up for a session.

RATING: 9
Excellent. The needs cards are a really interesting mechanic. I prefer this to Dungeon Lords.

For those who are curious about the differences, Dungeon Petz is worker placement + putting on a show. It's all about maximizing how best to prepare your pets for selling.

Dungeon Lords is action selection/programming + puzzle with a sprinkling of spatial building. It's all about preparing your dungeon to not be trampled by the incoming party.

RATING: N/A
In so many ways, Dungeon Petz is a signature Vlaada Chvatil design. It features a thoroughly original and funny theme, which is closely melded together with sound albeit somewhat heavy mechanics, and thus creates a very satisfying experience both on the level of game-play and from the perspective of a fun atmosphere. Some may find that the game is too heavy and ponderous for the whimsical theme, but most gamers who enjoy the challenges of deeper gameplay will find that the light-hearted elements of the game are just the redemptive qualities that they need in order to draw them into the mechanics and ensure that this clever and amusing game for gamers doesn’t overstay its welcome.

See my full review: http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/817366

RATING: 4
Dungeon Petz didn't ultimately work for me and my group as we found the amount of planning was undone by who got the right cards when the right exhibitions and buyers came out (even with the advanced rules). On one hand, managing the pets' needs was fun and thematic, on the other, the rest of the game bogged down in calculations and forward planning. I'm not sure what type of game this wants to be, and the blind worker placement "bidding" just seems gimmicky. What does that even represent anyway? 

In the end, there seems to be an incongruence in what the game wants itself to be. Too fiddly for something lighter and too luck-based to be strategic.

RATING: 7.8
Zman arriv 1-2012

Players: 2-4
Duration: 90 mins
Type: worker placement

I love the graphics and colors, and again the components are terrific, especially the pets. The mechanic for collecting cards is very unique, and I think the pet entertainment aspect is clever, as is an escaping pet, and the need to keep the cage clean. Very thematic. Planning ahead is vitally important. Rules are entertaining and well written.

My main complaint is that it is rule heavy, and therefore a hard game to pull out once in awhile.

We could not figure out what the arrows on the board mean which looks like an intended flow pattern--expansion? However, these "flow" arrows do not appear on the board pictured either on the bottom of the box or on the two page lay out in the rule book. I don't see a question about it in the forum either. (???)  EDIT: Note from Progmode to see sidebar on pg. 4--used when playing with less than 4 players. 

As in Dungeon Lords, the only player interaction is when you beat someone out of a placement. No fighting in this one. They are very different games, and I do prefer this one over Dungeon Lords.

RATING: 8.75
This game is so much fun to play! What other game is about growing cute little extravagant pets (or,  in this case, petz),  taking care of their needs and cleaning their poop :-) 
Shure there are pure euro style mechanics that support the overall functionality of the game system, so, many players could think this is another soulless euro plenty of dry mechanisms, but, let me tell you this from my humble opinion: those opinions are wrong.
Dungeon Petz is one masterpiece of modern boardgame design, because it achieves what, apparently, is a very difficult task: it integrates perfectly theme with mechanisms! 
Your lovely monstrously petz will suffer if you forget to care for their necessities, otherwise, they will be happy and will make you feel proud when gaining exhibitions... And yes, you even could feel sad when a costumer choose one of your little magical babies to share the rest of his/her life in a new home, far from your petz store. 
Yeah, those cuties are far from being dry soulless victory point generators. THEY'RE ALIVE! 
:-D

The bidding is interesting, but I always suck at blind bidding so it's not my favourite mechanic. I like that all of the actions are good and the restrictions guides the blind bidding nicely. It's an extremely interactive but still rather heavy euro, which is always a tough balance, but I think this game does it very well.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Dungeon Petz is the second of designer Vlaada Chvátil's two subversive takes on the worker placement genre. Like its predecessor Dungeon Lords, workers are committed in secret, without knowledge of what opponents' workers are doing. And in both games, the worker placement and resource collection is only a prelude to the brain-twisting "puzzle phase" where you must figure out how to effectively use those resources.

I like Dungeon Petz significantly more than its predecessor Dungeon Lords for several reasons. (1) The worker placement method of Dungeon Petz produces fiercer competition and a greater feeling of tension. (2) Dungeon Petz allows for more planning ahead, and that planning is more demanding and more interesting. (3) Dungeon Petz is an easier game to teach because the "puzzle phase" has less rules overhead. (4) For me at least, the theme of Dungeon Petz is more fun and more colorful. One of your clients, for example, hopes to see as many colors of poop as possible when he visits your store.

Overall, I submit that Dungeon Petz is more of a gamer's game than Dungeon Lords, requiring a greater degree of finesse and more intense competition. At the same time it has a cleaner design, with fewer and more sensible rules.

RATING: 6
Lovely artwork. There is one game mechanism that makes it work or break for you: The blind bidding that makes the outcome arbitrary. Either you don't get what you bidded for or you overpay it. Both results are very bad as there are only 5 rounds to play and any mistake will let you fall behind in a manner you won't catch up anymore. In Dungeon Lords you have a lot of options to choose but you can't take them all. In Dungeon Petz it is vice versa (you won't get them all). Some spots on the board are only available to one player each round. I am sorry to say but if the author believes making resources so scarce that this game should be still fun, he's wrong.
Furthermore this was the first game I had to deal with shit and cleaning it up. Once again: not as funny as the author believes...

D.A. Regalo de Yera 2014. 

Es un euro, de pensar mucho, y elegir correctamente las acciones y los recursos... cuidando de unos animalitos que parecen salidos de un cruce de pokemon con dungeons & dragons. 

si, original es un rato. y ademas de eso es un euro de esos de darle al coco a hervir y muy cuidado en las mecanicas. 

la tematica es chocante, y algunos elementos de la mecanica, tambien (hay que comprarles jaulas a los bichos cumpliendo caracteristicas tales como tenerlos entretenidos o retener su rabia, limpiar las caquitas - literalmente - y cosas similares). los bichos son muy monos (la debilidad de los niños... bueno, de aquellos a los que les gusten las cosas con cuernos, tentaculos y dientes puntiagudos). el juego en si, al principio no te terminas de creer que es un euro de esos duros al nivel de agricola o tzolkin... pero lo es, y hay que calcular y pensar muy bien las acciones para lograr la victoria final (basicamente, tener los bichos lo mejor cuidados posible, que no enfermen ni sufran, y que ganen exposiciones y los compren a buen precio). 

es un excelente euro, quiza no sea el mejor, pero si uno muy bueno, y muy original.

RATING: 10
I absolutely adore this game! It ticks all the boxes I look for in a game. It's pretty, complex and fun.

First of all, the graphic desing is the best I have ever seen and extremely detailed. All the pieces are absolutely beautiful starting from the box art. The rule book is colourful and immerses into the theme of the game explaining the rules in a humorous fashion that will surely put a smile on your face. This game is clearly a labour of love.

The gameplay might not be for casual gamers since there are tons of little rules that seem random at first but in reality everything makes perfect sense. For example, minions can be sent off to market place with money but money can not be sent alone since it doesn't have legs. Cages need at least 2 minions to carry them since they are heavy. When monsters at market aren't sold, they are discarded and mysteriously the amount of meat at sale is increased. Little details like this may not be everyone's cup of tea but I love them since they make the world of the game so rich and enhance the experience of dealing with monsters.

Also, on top of being detailed, the game can be brutally competitive when everyone is fighting for the same scarce resources. Another thing I love since most of my games are nice eurogames. If only you have a group of friends willing to take the time to memorise all the little rules then I can not recommend this enough. A masterpiece of a game!

The only cons I can think of is that the final scoring requires quite a lot of calculations which can be a bore. Also in between rounds there is a lot to do when you have to clean the market place to put new stuff in and make sure to bring your minions back from different tasks.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
There seems to be a trend in worker placement games lately towards giving each worker a different value, and allowing each worker to be further improved in value. Lancaster and Strasbourg are two that work in a similar fashion. Of those three, this seems to be the weakest game. However, the worker placement element is the strongest part of this game. It creates interesting decisions and even requires a bit of psychology in predicting both the value of your opponents' workers, as well as what resources they will want to take first. 

Unfortunately, once the workers are placed and resources are taken, players will spend a fair amount of time staring at their hands of cards and puzzling out the optimal way to proceed. There is randomness, but it shows up BEFORE you have to make any decisions. This may be good news to some people because then your decisions are about how to adapt to this randomness. However, I'd prefer the other way, where you make your best guess or take a risk, and then see how the randomness affects you. I don't say that as a general statement about all games, just this one in particular because of the downtime and silent puzzling it creates. There are actually two places in the rules where it essentially asks you very nicely to please take your turn quickly and to be patient with those who don't. Good advice in any game, but it's an indicator of a rampant problem here that I think could have been fixed with a different design choice.

Ultimately, if you like that puzzling aspect (which was also seen in Dungeon Lords), this may work for you. The randomness is a bit more controlled and the worker placement creates more decisions. It seems to play a bit faster too. I would just prefer to play something where the decisions are more intuitive rather than calculable.

RATING: 10
Last Played: 2010-2015
Plays: 5
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
For anyone who says an Euro doesn't need theme then they must experience Dungeon Petz. The mechanics and flavor are so perfectly amalgamated that they just build upon each other.

The theme impeccably supports the rules and brilliantly explains the mechanics (e.g. losing no points when you're unknown, bigger pets need more tending, etc). There isn't anything glaringly pure mechanics such as roundels, movement resource, color charts, etc. 

Likewise the well-throught gameplay rules build upon the theme (e.g. cannot remove suffering from pets, takes 2 imps to carry a cage, etc.) and all done without relying on chunks of flavor text or detailed miniatures. 

A benchmark example of masterful concoction of theme with mechanics that results in a cocktail that is greater than the sum of its parts.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.9
遊戲設置:
1.每位玩家一個檔板，10個小精靈，2個奴隸，寵物地城版，成就圖片
2.主圖板依照人數2-3人(格子之間有間隔)或4人那面
3.遊戲回合板塊依照人數設置(左下角有顯示)，4人共5回合，2-3人6回合
紫色回合標記物標在1的位置，每個人放置4個小惡魔在1~4下方各一隻(遠房親戚後來才用的到)
4.洗混展覽板塊，在中間欄位2~5回合個面著下放置一片，翻開第一個展覽板塊
5.洗混顧客板塊，在最底欄位3~5回合放置板塊，翻開第一片
****6.若2-3人進行遊戲，將未使用玩家的小惡魔放置在每個標有小點區得板塊，3人各區放一隻共3隻，2人各區往前數兩格再放一隻共6隻。另外2-3人，有4個額外行動區是虛擬行動區。
每回合這些中立小精靈會依箭頭前進一步。
7.寵物板塊洗混面朝下，抽三個面朝上放在寵物區下半部(2~3人抽兩個，1個被佔)，數值為2，抽一個面朝上放在寵物區上半部，數值為3。
8.寵物籠子洗混面朝下，翻開三個面朝上放置(兩人遊戲1個被佔)
9.籠子配件板塊洗混面朝下放置在主圖版旁，翻開兩片於配件區
10.寶物圓片洗混面朝下，翻開兩個面朝上放置於寶物區(兩人遊戲第一回合都被占領。)
11.食物綠色為蔬菜紅色為肉，全部放置在儲藏區(右上方)，然後補充攤販食物，綠色攤販蔬菜2，中間肉菜各1，下方2肉。(2-3人中間混合區被佔領)
12.需求牌四堆分顏色各自洗混朝下，預留棄牌區
13.將5張藥劑牌放在醫院區，痛苦、變異、糞便標記形成供應區
14.每位玩家奴隸一隻放在計分軌、一隻在展覽帳篷區，錢幣放置在中央方形銀行區域。

個人圖板:
1.每個人起始6隻小精靈，2元。出口為貨物採購放置，下方為食物儲藏區(上面蔬菜，下面肉)與寶物儲藏區。(蔬菜三回合會腐敗，肉兩回合)
2.起始籠子放置一個糞便標記，紅色數字代表耐久度，紫色為魔法抗性，每個籠子各有一個配件升級區
3.抽4張手牌，每種顏色一張，隱藏資訊

遊戲流程: 每回合6個階段
設置
1.眼睛：翻看下一回合的寵物展示會和商人牌(第一回合不用)
2寵物蛋+箭頭：
a.補上新的寵物蛋在下層(數值2)，每一個上層移除的寵物會變成1肉在肉攤
b.將剩餘籠子最下方保留並移置最上層，補充兩個新籠子
c.將剩餘聾子配件最下方保留並移置最上層化，補充一個新配件
d.還有補滿食物攤(不累加，依據新展示的寵物展覽板塊下方數字)，(2-3人時，如果中立小精靈在虛設行動區，該欄食物會少1。)，若有寵物被移除肉+1
e.寶物移除，放置新寶物
3.錢幣發薪:順位1、2的領1元，順位3、4領2元(2-3人，起始1元其他2元)
 採購
1.規劃分組:將小精靈與金幣分組放置，金幣不可以單獨，數量最多的由大到小排列放置在洞穴由左到右。
2.選擇行動: 由數量最多小組先行動，平手按順位，在依照數量第二多小組行動依此類推。
**可以選擇不執行行動，小精靈放回去錢收回，若放置行動，錢付給銀行，小精靈選擇一個區域全部放下去佔領執行
a.採購食物:得到該區食物，放置在個人圖板食物區最左邊
!!!b.購買籠子:須至少兩隻小精靈才能購買
(籠子可以到第三階段在選擇放置，若是替代籠子，標記會轉移到新的，但是籠子配件不會移動)
c.採買籠子配件:可以到第三階段在選擇放置
!!!d.購買寵物:必須要有金幣才購買，依據小精靈放置上下區域購買，寵物可以先放在空的區域
e.邀請新的小精靈:將該回合數以前小精靈招募回家，下回使用
f.醫院行動:將已經住院的小精靈移到放置區域，回合結束可以領走，另外抽取一張藥劑卡。
g.展覽志工:可以將展覽帳篷的奴隸往前多走兩步
!!!h.預定展覽台(加分):小精靈本回合不會回來，會移動到右邊區域預備，當你出售寵物並使用展覽台加分時，小精靈一次返回一隻(每回合出售一隻)。
 需求(按步驟但可以各玩家同時進行)
1. 安排籠子與寵物:之前隻籠子與配件不可以移動，新得到的籠子可以放到空地或取代舊的，寵物可以移動至其他籠子，糞便標記會留下，其他標誌會跟著寵物移動。寵物沒籠子會丟失。
2.抽需求牌:根據寵物底下的彩色條顏色抽取對應顏色與數量的需求牌。
3.分配需求牌:根據寵物底下的彩條顏色分配需求牌。
(變體規則:卡片使用兩個需求標記，一次可以滿足兩項)
(醫院行動取得的藥劑牌可以取代任何一個需求)
 展覽
1.結算需求: 參考個人版圖右上角快速表
a.食物:依照寵物食性，給予菜/肉/雜食，每缺一個拿一個痛苦標記(##若寵物痛苦標記超過寵物本身彩條數量，寵物會死掉)
(如果籠子有幾個葉子/肉的標記就可以抵幾個葉子/肉需求。)
b.糞便:依照數量增加糞便，籠子有標記可抵一個。
(交易階段結束時，若個人地城區有小精靈會將糞便打掃乾淨，小精靈只能打掃空的籠子，每隻可以清掉兩個糞便，可不同籠子)
c.玩樂:一個玩樂標記需要一隻小精靈，小精靈可以一次滿足籠子相鄰的寵物(不能對角相鄰)，若籠子有玩樂符號可以抵一次需求。少一隻小精靈就領一個痛苦標記。
d.憤怒:憤怒的需求總數若小於等於籠子強度(紅色數字)沒事，若憤怒需求超過會有兩種可能1.每超過一點需要消耗一隻小惡魔進醫院。2.若沒有小惡魔，寵物會消失，並且失去分數。
e.魔法:魔法的需求總數若小於等於籠子魔法抗性沒事(紫色數字)，若超過發生變異，每超過一點拿一個變異標記，寵物累積兩點變異會消失，並且失去分數。
f.疾病:疾病總數+糞便少於2沒事，超過2點拿N-1痛苦標記(ex:3點拿2個，4點拿3個)
##當寵物有用藥劑牌抵某種顏色的需求時，需要棄掉該顏色一張
2.展覽計分
a.當前展覽計分板塊(事前就會得知)，右上角為加分項目，中間為扣分項目。
b.單一寵物計分(展覽板塊左上角為一個籠子標記)，更據需求牌與展覽卡搭配，選一隻計分最高的寵物算分。
c.全體寵物算分(展覽板塊左上角為4個籠子標記)，所有個人寵物版上寵物都計分(包括空的籠子)。
d.根據展覽分數獲取聲望值。第一名8點，第二名6點，第三名4點，第四名2點(展覽分數至少不為0)，若平手各得當名次分數減去每多一個並列扣1點。(例如兩個第一名各得7點)
######失去寵物######
a.沒籠子養、受苦標記超過、憤怒超過沒有小惡魔、變異標記超過
以上情況發生失去寵物。(寵物、牌、標記移除遊戲、糞便留下)
b.每有10點聲望就到扣一點。
 交易
1.出售一隻寵物
a.根據顧客喜好，上方加分(若有彩色要看卡牌顏色一次只能算一組)，右下角扣分。
b.所得到分數要大於0分，且寵物體型要大於四。(寵物轉四次)才能出售。
c.選擇黑市交易或展覽台交易。黑色交易所得的分數X2變成聲望點數。展覽台交易必須在主圖版展覽區有至少一隻小精靈，當寵物售出後該小精靈移動到該寵物空的籠子。所得到分數X3。
每個回合只能使用一次展覽台交易(包括最後回合兩名顧客選一位)
d.獲得金幣，獲得寵物上標示的金幣，若有變異符號扣2元。
2.棄掉所有分配好的需求牌於棄牌堆。
3.使用個人地城版圖上的小精靈。每隻可以清理空的籠子2個糞便，或者獲得1元。
 成長
1.寵物成長: 根據寵物箭頭指示轉動寵物條。最後一回合不用。
2.食物變質:所有食物向右移動一格，最右邊棄掉。
3.收回小惡魔:收回寵物版上小惡魔，收回主圖板上小惡魔，除了展覽台上和醫院區的，展覽台預備上的小精靈向右移至展覽台。
4.起始玩家向左邊傳，最後一回合給聲望分數最低玩家。(2-3人都只要向左傳。)
####最終計分####
1.金幣0.5分，每個食物標記和每個寶物1分，若最後有小精靈在醫院區或展覽台每隻扣2分。
2.每隻在寵物版上的2分，每個籠子與配件各1分，若有糞便、苦難、變異標誌每個扣1分。
####寶物敘述####
1.書本:(立即)從寶物上兩種顏色選其一，抽一張額外該顏色手牌。
2.水晶球:抽需求牌前，先從手上至多棄三張，再從牌庫一起補。
3.鏟子:可以在展覽階段前派小精靈清楚寵物上的糞便2個。將小精靈放到寵物版圖正中間，清除的糞便放到鏟子上做標記，回合結束清掉，每個大回合可以使用一次。
4.小精靈鎧甲:最多兩隻小精靈可以使用，放到該籠子紅色或紫色數字上，視為+1該屬性，且小精靈不會送去醫院。
5.最佳員工:1個小精靈能力X2，在清籠子、陪寵物玩、捕捉寵物時，可以combo其他寶物。例外:若小精靈沒使用不能一次換2元。
6.服從之鞭:寵物展覽上額外獲得0.5分，奴隸多前進半步。
7.魔法冰箱:第一次獲得時候可以從公用區拿一個食物放在該寶物上，該食物可以代表任務種類食物，若使用完了可以將自己在腐爛區內的食物移一個上來。

RATING: 7.5
2012-06-13

Initial Rating: 6.0 (June 2012)

As usual, I was the rules reader of my game group, however I made a BIG mistake teaching and playing the game so my first game was awful. I need to play this again soon to reevaluate my rating. I had very high hopes for this one because it’s a Vlaada design but maybe this will be his first game I don´t like. 

The theme is too cute for me, in Dungeon Lords the theme was funny but darker, here, it continues to be funny but it is too sweet for my taste and for what I was expecting. The rules, as always, were hilarious. It seems to be a lighter game than Dungeon Lords.

For now I prefer to play Pret a Porter or Vinhos over this one.

Current Rating: 6.0
[hr]
2014-02-13

After more plays, I have come to appreciate this game a lot! Vlaada strikes again :D. The theme has won me over and now I enjoy it completely, from the art, to the rulebook, to your lair. Everything is great. This is a worker placement game with a fresh take, I LOVE that you need to bid with minions and gold for the order of each action in the game. I also really like how the players get involved in the game, if a pet suffers, the owner of that pet also suffers. This makes for a fun game as you need to be observing your enemies and, thus, I don’t feel any downtime. With the random disposition of pets and dungeon lords (thus how much meat and vegetables appear each round), the game has a lot of replay value. For now, each game has felt different. 

Dungeon Petz has a lot of tension and interesting decisions with any number of players (although I prefer to play it with 4).

Still I prefer Dungeons Lord over this one but it`s been awhile since I played DL so my opinion can change.

Bottom line, a very good worker placement and a game I want to buy in the future. I won’t say no to a game of Dungeon Petz. A true gamer´s game.

RATING: 7
Dungeon Petz is in many ways a typical Vlaada Chvatil game.  There is a basic problem to be solved - to keep pets happy, healthy and valuable - and a worker placement phase not to mention many detailed rules to determine exactly how players go about solving it.

This game's greatest innovation is the bidding system for worker placement.  I am not a fan of blind bidding as a general rule, and neither are most of the gamers I play with.  However, the ability to group one's workers to maximize either quality or quantity of actions is a brilliant twist on the standard worker placement action draft.

Truthfully I didn't find myself inspired by much of the rest of the game.  The shows for buyers and dungeon lords might be interesting but instead I found myself with a pet mismatched to their interests, through what I perceived as no fault of my own since I couldn't see their interests yet.

I suspect that, like Dungeon Lords, this game won't really shine on a first play since the rules weight is so heavy.  Many Vlaada games are worth playing repeatedly because there is a great experience inside.  I don't intend to seek out that experience with Dungeon Petz since even though it may be better than Dungeon Lords I've more plays invested in the earlier title.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The blind bidding portion of separating your imps and gold is simply ok.  As expected, there can be a steep risk/reward aspect to making too small or large of a group.  Overall, it works fine, but that's not the selling piece of the game.

What is?  The pet's 'needs' mechanic!  Very, very elegantly put together.  There are 4 decks of cards - each dedicated to one of four primary needs a pet may have.  Green (food/poop), Red (anger), Yellow (play), and Purple (Magic).  You will ultimately decide what your pet's needs are, but as it's card-driven, you may not always draw what you'd expect.  Very thematic!  Each pet has tendencies, but as card decks tend to do, they illustrate how your pet can have a mind of its own by not behaving as advertised.  This really is a great thematic component to the game.

Points, or Reputation, is primarily scored via exhibitions and selling your pets.  Both, exhibitions and customers, offer reputation based on your pets' needs on a specific turn.  So there is a balancing act between getting points via exhibitions, getting points via selling pets to customers, and finally maintaining a healthy pet and clean cage.

It adds up to a relatively light, but very thematic and fun 'take care of your pet' game.  The only downside is the amount of time required to teach a newbie.  While it is quite intuitive once you 'get it', you have no choice but to explain it all in order to 'get there'.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Very good game! It really presents you with lots of choices that are quite agonizing but also a lot of fun. The theme is adorable, although not all the game mechanics really feel thematic, the overall cuteness of tending to monstrous pets is really appealing to me.

There is a lot of worker placement and this is where one of the clever game mechanics comes in. Players have imp workers and everyone secretly sorts their imps into groups behind their player screens. For example I might arrange mine into a group of 3, two groups of 2 and one group of 1. Players can also use gold to add an imp of strength to the group. This is almost the only use of gold. Once everyone has their groups they are revealed simultaneously and whoever has the largest group places first, ties going to the start player and left-wise thereof. So it's a kind of hybrid bidding worker placement thing. Kind of like a lot of different games but unique in it's own way.

The other really nice mechanic is the tending of pets. Each pet must be assigned a certain number of cards that are of specific colours. This happens each round and players draw all of these cards just before assigning them. They will also have one of each colour in hand from the previous round. Then assignments must be made. Depending on what cage your pet is in and how much food and what customers might be coming you try to optimize the allocation of the cards. This is usually very painful, in a good way.

I really enjoy the game a lot. It is hard like Vlaada's other big games but it doesn't feel as heavy or intricate. I like Dungeon Lords but I never fell in love with it because of how complicated the combat was. It often felt like a puzzle (much like Mage Knight does) however Dungeon Petz feels nothing like this. You have hard decisions but they don't weigh you down too much.

The rule book is a bit long but the game doesn't play like it, so if that's scaring you away or the fact that you didn't enjoy Vlaada's other big games I would give this one a try.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I need to play this again but...

I left feeling slightly frustrated with the game. It appears to offer a lot of choices but i'm not entirely sure that those choices had a great deal of impact on the scoring. While the action selection mechanism is ok I think that too much importance is placed on procuring the 'right' pet to be able to sell to the Dungeon Lords - if you miss out then you will struggle to make up points elsewhere. Added to this is that you will often have to take a pet 'blind' and grow it for 2 seasons before selling, but at the point of sale you have no idea if it will be worth anything.

So all in all, i'm not rushing back to this one.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
2011 was the year of exhibition-themed games (Vinhos, Drum Roll, Pret-a-Porter, Air Show, Pergamon) and Dungeon Petz is the highest-ranked of them. Chvatil again managed the trick to create a Eurogame that ranks high amongst thematic gamers and as of writing this comment is #20 in thematic games on BGG.
On the mechanical (Euro) side, it's a worker placement game with one central simultaneous decision in how many groups players want to divide up their workers. For example, six workers can be split up in six singles for the maximum amount of actions, two groups of three workers each or any other combination. The larger the group, the earlier it acts. This mechanism coupled with very limited action spots works perfectly.
On the thematic side, players have to fulfil the needs of monsters they nurture. Each turn, players caringly reveal the cards they have associated with each monster and tell small stories. The successful thematic integration helps to get beyond the processional and sometimes drawn out flow of the game.
Victory is decided in best fulfilling the demands of exhibitions and buyers. There's some luck in getting the right cards at the right time but enough possibilities to mitigate or at least reduce adverse effects.
Nice enough game although I'm not sure if it really is a 7 for me or if I'm a bit generous due to winning the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The core of the game is matching up needs (behaviour) with requirements on one hand and demand on the other. Requirements are collected through a semi-blind action drafting procedure. Demands come in two different types of set which may or may not match each other in any particular round. The catch is that you can only profit to the extent that your cards allow you to bridge the gap between capacity and demand. There are ways to reduce the luck of the draw, but not everyone will have access to these (or everyone will have just a little). So it's possible to set up a potentially huge payoff only to miss out because instead of drawing manageable, profitable cards you draw non-manageable, non-profitable ones and have to bend over double just to avoid penalties that would reduce your role in the game to occasional hate drafter. Three players worked fine, but for each player below four, the drafting procedure takes a hit from the scripted action blocking. Playing time is OK. Ambience is endearing but I can see how it might give some people an allergic reaction.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
I'm not much of a fan of most of Chvatil's work - I often find his games unforgiving without being fun, procedural without being exciting, and/or pressurized without any sense of real accomplishment. Well, score one for Vlaada - Dungeon Petz is by far my favorite of his games I've played. It's rewarding, it's reasonably easy to pick up, it plays in a bit less time than Dudgeon Lords or Through the Ages. 

But I think what I like most, what makes this game so much better - and this is faintly ridiculous - is the ultrafun theme. Who doesn't want to be a monster salesman? It's really entertaining that there's an ogre that wants some of your animals for manure, and a lich who thinks animals that eat are gross. For some reason it feels less like a cascading series of disasters than its similarly themed predecessor, even though it actually is just as tough and finicky. For some reason, you can just take it less seriously when you're raising a gigantic magical monster than when you're protecting your shit from being stole by armored ruffians.

As with any Vlaada game, it can get fiddly at times, but my family and I love this game. I enjoy the myriad choices I have to make, and the fact that there is not one clear path to victory.

For instance, in a three player game in which the other players were fighting it out for the platform, I spent my imps and gold maximizing my performance on the last two auctions and won. Great game!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Under the cutesy art is a fairly arid, heavy and traditional euro.  The whole idea isn't that complex, but there are *a lot* of rules and a lot of time is spent 'running' the game.  Looking at the player aid (at the top of the player boards) is enough to discourage even reasonably courageous gamers.  The novel thing is variable worker placement where you have to balance priority with number of placements.  Relatively few of the available spots are really valuable at any given point and competition is very (almost too) stiff (with 4 players at least).

Our 1st game took 3 hours, and I would say the weight is higher than Dungeon Lords (same designer).  Additionally, it's a completely different game so don't make too many assumption about ties between the two.  I rarely say this, but this felt a bit too heavy for it's own good.  The stakes and overall concept/workings would probably be better supported by more streamlined proceedings.

Still a good, if heavy, euro that's worth looking into if that's your kind of things.  But do not be fooled by the cute cuddly child-friendly pets, this isn't a kids game, it ain't light, and it flows like slime.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
This is a worker placement, card management game with some angst in the decision making.  You certainly want to avoid competing in the type of food other players need if possible as that is one way players can really be nasty.  One has to manage poop, feed their pets, play with them, maintain their magical abilities without going too far and allowing them to mutate, and keep the angry pets in their cages.  The key aspect to the game is managing your pets needs which is managed through the five types of card where there is a certain type of distribution of the different needs.  This game is certainly much more accessible than the predecessor Dungeon Lords which can be extremely painful to teach.

RATING: 9
Yesssss!  Another Vlaada Chvatil title. 'Nuff said.

Well, I can say more. This is a cute game, light, and unpredictable in a fun way. You do your best, but sometimes you just can't get your pet to behave for that sale! (you might just draw the wrong needs cards, even with a statistical advantage)

The game flows very well, except for one thing. The immigration line. That's where you get your new workers. You have a silent bid for first move each turn, effectively. If a player gets outbid just a little bit by different people each turn, she can get shut out of workers the whole game and that can be harsh.

RATING: 8
"Que bonito es ese mapache papa, quiero uno". esta frase se repite todos los dias (bueno, 1 vez al mes o al año) delante de alguna tienda de mascotas en el mundo.

Que ocurriría si en vez de humanos, ¿fueramos demonios y señores del mal? tenemos acaso la exclusiva? la respuesta es NO. Ellos tambien necesitan de pequeños seres de los que preocuparse, entrenar a su imagen y semejanza e incluso presumir de ellos delante de los demas. Pero, como ocurre tal proceso? ¿quien se encarga de que esto pueda ser posible?

este hecho es el que resuelve Dungeon Petz. 

Tomamos el rol de un grupo de goblins, conocedores de las exigencias de los señores infernales. Deben conseguir, criar y cuidar todo tipo de criaturas que enamoren a los mas insignificantes y petreos corazones y conseguir asi un negocio altamente lucrativo.

Compite ante un jurado (o sobornalos), construye jaulas para criar a tan bellas criaturitas demoniacas.

RATING: 7.954

RATING: 8
Great theme, excellent mechanics. Eurogame, so very little interaction.

The rules are deceptively easy to learn. The rulebook is well written and fun to read. The seeming complexity of the game is greatly alleviated by very good theme integration - the rules thus feel very intuitive. This game is excellent even for some non-gamers.

I am not very good at worker placement games. Thus, theme becomes crucial to me. I play to win, but even losing badly can be fun with Dungeon Petz.

RATING: 8
Having the manic little imps from Dungeon Keeper compete in the raising and selling of variously cute pets is so great an idea that it could have survived on somewhat derivative mechanics. That’s not how Chaatvil designs his games, apparently, and so the great idea and the accompanying great artwork come with a new twist on worker placement, where you essentially perform a blind bit for placement priority, and a system of determining the needs of your beasties that is very thematic and feels quite fresh, too.

Judging from our first run, it all works together very well, underpinning the enthusiastic sales pitches, the pet show jury-rigging and the (uniformly bad) poo jokes that were flying around our table with a satisfying range of strategic options and round-to-round finagling of plans. Especially recommended if you liked Dungeon Lords, since the games play very differently whilst being of similar weight and luck/strategy balance, I’d say.

RATING: 5
Huge disappointment! This game seemed to have so much promise. Unfortunately, it fell flat and ultimately became frustrating for 3 reasons.

1. It's fiddly--there is a setup/replenishment phase in every round (5 times including original setup). Having to check off each area to see if it's setup right gets easier as you go, but is somewhat of a pain.
2. Too many rules--There are just too many seemingly unnecessary rules, which add unnecessary complexity. The fact that there are SEVERAL "special" rules to scale for 2 and 3 players exacerbates this problem. The theme is light and playful, but the ruleset is dense and overbearing. Not only is this not a gateway, I wouldn't play this with anyone other than a committed gamer. That alone is unfortunate and limiting. 
3. It's frustrating--In a game of this weight, players should never have to grapple with so much randomness. The pet draws are random, the artifact draws are random, the add-on and cage draws are random, the card draws are VERY random and can be damaging. Also, the customer tile and exhibit tiles are, at least later in the game, unknown and random. This randomness combined with the tightness of the game, create a frustrating experience. On a side note, I believe that the artifact spot is overpowered. 

The game was a huge bust. I didn't even find the bidding part, the part I though I would enjoy most, very interesting at all. The components are okay. I like the minions and some of the tokens, but I wish the cardboard was thicker. The iconography is okay, but takes some time to get used to. The one silver lining in this game is the theme. It integrates well into the mechanics, for sure. Unfortunately, I just didn't find the compelling enough to outweigh the negatives.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
The theme and theme-integration were the selling points of this game for me, and it works very well in that regard. However, I care very little for theme, only that it's there (I get bored with games that are completely abstract in terms of components, as far as gameplay I couldn't care at all). My plan for this game was to try out a more complex euro with my wife who's very theme-sensitive and needs it to be able to connect with and bother to play a game at all. It worked as I said, she really bought into the whole pet shop aspect and enjoyed it immensely. As for me, I enjoy it, it's a neat puzzle, the blind-bidding phase where you bid for priority in the action selection phase (worker placement phase) is neat because it's not really "solveable". It's risk vs reward all the way  and it brings with it a lot of chaos when there's more than two players. Then after that it's sort of well... It's a very good and tricky puzzle. All sorts of probability calculation for the cards you are going to draw, and you need to draw up contingency plans and to consider consequences for at least the two following turns. Which competitions will you win, how to maximize your sales, will you have to bother with food or can you take a misery token to grab that artifact instead? Very puzzly, and then you get the cards - or "needs". You see, every animal you have needs something, the older (and more valuable) it is, the more it needs. These are represented by several decks of cards with a known distribution.

So there you sit. Three older animals in your pen, perhaps two workers who stayed home to help mitigate "unfortunate" circumstances, a bit of food - not enough if they all decide to be hungry, an artifact that will maybe help you if they decide try to mutate into gooey blobs. Then you get a fistful of cards and the game says, solve this! So you sit, and all the other players, sit and shuffle their cards, some crying out in frustration as they see it just won't work - something has to give. Others gleefully complete in a second, their pets so well behaved there was no challenge at all. Then when everyone has done their best, you resolve the need piles. This is where you realize that you miscounted, and what you thought would turn out just fine turns into mayhem with Animals fleeing, mutating into goo, dying of misery and disease. No money for you haha!

Ultimately, it's a portion of the game something like Galaxy Truckers resolution phase, it's simply a lot more fun when it goes really really awry. Unfortunately, it's a bit too controllable for it to happen all that often.

So where does this leave the game? Well, it's just kind of solitaire? You all do your things, then you hopefully get to laugh at the others mistakes - but it doesn't happen often enough for me. It's fun to do, but I'd rather have it even more silly (ala Galaxy Trucker), or I'd have a different much more interactive less puzzle-ish instead. So it leaves it a bit in the middle.

A good game, but I find myself reaching for either something more interactive, or something more silly, and for two players - where I bought it to shine, it's just a bit too long playtime compared to Patchwork / Agricola: All Creatures Big and Small, and a bit to long to set up. If me and my wife played these slightly longer two player games, this would shine much more.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.9
As usual with [b][person=789][/person][/b] games and specially this being on the [b][thing=45315][/thing][/b] universe, this is simply fantastic.

Every mechanic makes perfect thematic sense, everything is understood when someone is explaining what the actions are, what a player can and can't do, it all makes perfect thematic sense and that helps hugely to the understanding of a complex game.

This is not as complex as Dungeon Lords but it's as hard as it is when it comes to make choices and decisions on what to do and what to do first... it's just amazing... I always feel that agonizing pain of needing to do this, that and that other things and trying to deduce what others will be going for, how much should I "bid" trying to get to the first action first, ect, etc, etc...

When I'm playing I have the feeling that a Spiritual Vlaada is hanging over the game and Evil Laughing at the agony that all players are suffering at the table! Vlaada is a Genius... an Evil one for sure!

In terms of components the game is just top notch, everything is super high quality.

The art is just adorable, the pets are so cute, the board is amazing with so many wonderful little details like the some Imps playing Dungeon Lords in one corner...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
A nice departure from the common "goods production" type Euro (not that those are bad, there's just a lot of them). The theme, as many have said, is fantastic and the art is vibrant and nice to look at throughout. Paired with that is interesting and dynamic gameplay. Some might see the theme as childish (the z in dungeon petz probably isn't doing it any favors), but it holds the game together very well.

Many have spoke of it being hard to learn/understand, but I think that only applies if you are making the jump from carcassonne/7 wonders type games. If you're used to games like agricola, puerto rico, etc., then this won't be hard at all. There's a lot going on but players can do fairly well without considering every little detail (e.g. future competitions/sellers or the distribution of cards of each type). While this isn't the perfect gateway game, it could work as one if the "newbie" is prepared for it. Some of the things in the game are a bit hard to explain, but once you're doing the actions everything is pretty easy to understand. That said, there is a lot of depth and room for interesting strategy, if that's your thing.

There is decent player interaction (through bidding for action priority and "contests"), though it's not very adversarial (rahdo likes DP, so that must be true). 

The mechanics in this game feel almost as unique as the theme. Assigning need cards to pets provides a satisfying "puzzle" every round which sometimes feels like juggling chainsaws (in a good way). As your pets grow older they acquire more and more needs, which ramps up the game in a fun way. The bidding for turn order sometimes feels formulaic (as in, everyone bids the same) but as the game goes on this seems to diverge (especially as players gain coins to bid with).  

There is luck, and it can screw you, but for me it does its job as being "deal with the hand you're dealt" luck. Serious gamers who want to plan obsessively and refuse to "roll with the punches" might get frustrated, but I think most will enjoy the variety and tension it adds to the game.

I've only played with 2 players so I can't speak for 3 or 4, but the length seems perfect (full, but not achingly long; around 2 hours) and the dummy system with rotating imps who block action spaces works well. That said, I think I'd like 3 or 4 players where more spots are open.

Dungeon Petz is a fresh mid-weight euro with an out-of-left-field theme and clever, well-designed mechanics.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
A fun, crazy simulation of raising pets.  The game is very representational: much of the charm comes from the game's ability to make you feel like you're raising these pets.  You want them to flourish!  For this game to work, you need to connect with these thematic elements.  

Mechanically, the worker placement mechanisms are a bit wonky.  But some tension is supposed to come from a push-your-luck decision of whether to use workers now of save them for animal needs in the following round.  

Dealing with animal needs is the real charming heart of the game.  It just works - even though it plays out as a completely solitaire card placement activity.   

I think the art style is too busy and saturated.  It is beautiful, but hard on the eyes.

RATING: 8
A gorgeous looking game thanks to David Cochard illustrations. The game rules are long and dense, so do not plan a game without reading them before and play solo a few turns. Otherwise you will spend more than 1 hour reading the rules and loosing the interest of other players (I did that mistake...).
Once playing the rules are much more clearer and logical so the apparent complexity does not translate in complicated game turns, even if there is still a lot to do and to remember.
It's quite typical of Vlaada Chvátil gamedesign, you would have the same feeling with Dungeon Lords.
So if you already know Dungeon Lords and don't like it, you probably won't like Dungeon Petz either. If you enjoy Dungeon Lords you will probably enjoy Dungeon Petz :)

RATING: 7.5
From the same designer of Dungeon Lords, this new game is themed on you running a pet store. You send imps to go shopping, to buy cages, pets and other items for your store. In certain rounds, you have to exhibit your pets and sell your pets. VPs are earned from the exhibitions and selling. 

Every round, you have to manage the needs of your pets, based on card draws. They may have play, poop, magic, disease needs and you have to try to manage all of them. 

It's a fun game, and similar feel to Dungeon Lords in that you are trying to minimize the pain you inflict on your pets. It's a bit more forgiving than Dungeon Lords, where in that game you could lose a bunch of tiles when monsters attack you. Here you would lose a pet, worst case scenario. Dungeon Lords is more fun for me, but this game is good too.

RATING: 9
The artwork is excellent. I was so busy enjoying the game that I didn't get to appreciate it all, but it's a bit like a Richard Scarry book - there's so much going on all over the board.
I didn't read the rules myself, but as explained to me, they made so much sense, actually more than I expected from a glance at the rules.

It wasn't too heavy, and it didn't induce too much brain hurt or AP - it seemed relatively straightforward to prioritise characteristics based on what was coming up on exhibition or customer tiles.
All in all, I loved it! A 9 for now, but will probably go higher (3-p game).

RATING: 8.5
Interesting worker-placement game. Dungeon Lords is in the same genre, but Dungeon Petz is a totally different take on that genre. Players bid on what order to place their workers by assembling "shopping teams" out of their imps and gold in secret, and then revealing them. The largest teams go first, and then the next largest, and so on, but the size doesn't matter for the action itself.

This, combined with highly limited placement options, makes for interesting strategy. Not only do you have to decide how much you value placing early, you have to figure out how much others value it and try to make your biggest team bigger than theirs, but only by a very small amount.

Contains great humor in the rulebook, but also in the mechanics. Also has fun moral decisions! Do you take great care of the horribly adorable baby monsters you're raising, or do you run a puppy mill and ride their suffering all the way to victory? How much poop do you allow to pile up in your monsters' pens before spending the time and resources to clean it up?

Definitely a fun game.

RATING: 5

Theme is nice and well implemented.

The reason that it doesn't score higher for me is that there is more tactic than strategy: I prefer games that offer more "freedom" and "creativity" in the strategy realm. In Dungeon Petz there are interesting choices, but more of the calculable sort and more "scripted". The customers card for example determine the way you play in great  amount, there is still some "freedom", but not enough for my taste.

RATING: 9
Fun, strategic, cute and humorous.

The detailed artwork on everything is amazing and attractive.  Just by looking at the box and the components, both my girlfriend and my nephew wanted to play it.

It is a Vlaada Chvatil game, so it is a bit complex, but far friendlier than Mage Knight.  Actually, you can just follow the rulebook's description phase after phase and do relatively well.  A couple of turns in and every player has a good idea of how to take care of the pets and how to use their imps.  That's because every action is pretty thematic and makes sense in the imp world.

I was disinterested in this game because I thought it was an expansion to Dungeon Lords, which felt too puzzly for me, but after watching Rahdo's runthrough, I knew I was going to like it a lot.

Try it!

RATING: 8
A worker placement and card game where you are trying to raise cute little deadly pets to sell or to compete in beauty competitions.  The interesting mechanism in this game is if your pet has 3 anger cards, in that pull of anger cards, some of the cards allow you to assign something else to the pet.  For instance, you have a pet that has 3 anger in a cage that can withstand 1 anger, 1 poop, and 1 magic.  You will be hoping that the three anger cards you draw are NOT all anger or else your pet will escape the pen and run away.

During the game there are places to go that will buy new cages, power-ups, first player, beauty contest, selling, and a few other spots I think.

The game is reminiscent of Dungeon lords, but there is PLENTY to distinguish this from the first.  Good game, a tad complicated, but definitely worthy of a spot in my collection.
