=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Not many games are about breeding, showing off and selling pets, the less about dwarves doing it. Even more improbable is that these pets are living and rather mercurial creatures that want food and games, get angry, can even mutate and obviously have their own biological needs. Well, here all of the above apply: players are in charge of a growing ensemble of dwarves that must be grouped in several units in each turn so they take care of buying cages and pets and then cater for all of the creatures' needs (represented by different groups of cards). Each pet has got needs of their own, and they can change from one game to another.

As the unlikely hodgepodge of different game mechanics that it is (blind auctions for grouping the dwarves, worker placement for fulfilling all the tasks, hand management for the pets' whims, penalties, a not really trivial scoring system for exhibitions and sales), Dungeon Petz takes several tries to sink in. Once it does, it never fails. The nicely interwoven random elements in the game result in considerable replayability, especially taking into account the amount of material that is used. Actually, the sturdiness of the design is a given for Chvátil, and so is the quirky humour.

RATING: 7
I finally got a chance to play this. Not sure what my response to it is. My initial reaction to hearing about it was "must buy", as I love Vlaada and we really enjoy Dungeon Lords. But when I had a chance to buy it, I passed. That's not much of an indication, as I also passed on [thing=70149][/thing] - not because I don't want it, but because I have a lot of unplayed games piling up, and I'd like to play more of those.

Anyway, the blind bidding aspect was a slight turn off. Vlaada's games always turn out a bit fiddly, and this one was no exception, but i think [thing=34887][/thing] does the blind bidding a little better and a little more fun. There is an obvious mechanical difference being that you are choosing turn order rather than actions. But since the value of actions is largely fixed, and everyone needs stuff, you're also bidding on those actions. I think if the actions were more equal, then you'd have some variation, but I didn't really see it. Of course, I only had one go at it. This is an example of making a game out of an slightly different action selection mechanism - worker placement with bidding for turns. The rest of the game could be anything. This just happens to be one mechanic I don't care for as much.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Dungeon Petz works mostly because of its theme, which is very well executed. Mechanically it's a bit odd - the concept of assigning a 'sickness' need takes some mind bending at first - but it makes more sense after a few rounds.

Interactive worker placement with a pre-planned / simultaneous / secret splitting of your force into groups of different sizes. Bigger groups get to go earlier but leave you with fewer groups overall. Send your imps forth to do all kinds of things: Buy food, get artifacts, impress the judges, buy new petz, buy new gear for your shop, etc. Grow your petz and show them off, hopefully sell them for points and profit too. Card / hand management is key to keeping your petz happy, since you have only a rough idea of what their needs/issues will be each round - the cards can and will surprise you.

Overall a winner for me. Just so much FUN to play, it makes up for a lot of fiddliness / gameyness in the design.

RATING: 8
心得:
可選擇的動作有點太少 
處理寵物的問題隨機性也比較高
想要贏應該主要是在某次能賣很高分就可以吧
規則:
*每回合會開一張競賽跟客戶卡，起始時就都開最左邊的，最後兩個客戶卡一起開
*每回補充的籠子數目跟寵物數目為可拿數目加一
*起始籠子有一個大便，六工人，兩塊錢，四種需求卡各一
*每回合一開始拿錢(1-1-2-2,1-2-2,1-2)
*中立玩家在紅色或綠色食物格時，中間混和食物會少一個對應食物
*沒買神器清空補新的，一踩就是兩個一起拿
*捕籠子跟擴展都是把最下面的拿到最上格(其他清掉)，在補新的
*寵物下層長大一級移到上層，下層在補三個，上層移掉多一個肉類食物
*每回合放寵物前可調整位置不用跟上回合一樣住同一個
*只能清空籠子大便，一次清兩個，可在不同籠子，如果工人沒用產一塊錢
*生病符號+大便數>2時，寵物會生病，得到(生病符號+大便數-1)的痛苦指示物。也就是總和為2時沒事，總和為3時有2個痛苦，總和為4時有3個痛苦。 
*最後面是兩個競賽，不是直接算分
*最右邊那格食物再往右移才是爛掉。換句話說，菜可保存3回合，肉可保存2回合
*神器
 水晶球 階段3 棄三張需求牌 抽三張
 鏟子 可在階段4任意時候清大便，
 盔甲 可用工人擋攻擊跟魔法
 最佳員工 一隻工人當兩隻用(清大便，陪寵物玩，擋攻擊)，不能產兩金
 長鞭 +0.5每個展覽，除了最後兩個
 魔法盒 拿到時裡面有一個萬用食物，吃完後之後可以在隨時存一個，且變成萬用，不會壞掉

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Super theme, a mass of components, fun rule book, interesting twists on old mechanics ... there's a lot to like. It's worker placement, but novelly done, in that rather than send one out each time your turn comes around to grab the things you need, you decide ahead of time how you choose to band your imps together in groups. The biggest groups still only get one placement but go first in order. So do you want two things desparately (create 2 big groups) or you're ok with latter picks but you want more things (smaller groups). Good decisions to make, and the normal tension of worker placement is ramped up a little. You're competing for pets and then all the things you need to keep your individual pet alive - food, cages, left over imps to play with it, etc. If you fail, it gets suffering points and then doesn't compete well in the Best Of Show each round, nor might it do well when you're selling it off. So balancing all these things provides an entertaining experience with a nice blend of inter-player competition plus the "sort out your pet by yourself" bit which is reminiscent of its brother game Dungeon Lords.

RATING: 4
What I liked:
- The theme carries through pretty well (but see below)
- Nice components

What I didn't like:
- The theme is depressing, as it is basically a cruel puppy mill (or prison)
- The core mechanism (imp placement) requires you to commit resources without enough information to do so
- It feels pointlessly complex to me (which is typical of Vlaada games)
- It is long (3p learning game aborted 2/3 complete after 3.5 hours including an hour of rules/setup)
- The card draws seem to add a *lot* of randomness for a game that feels so deep and thinky

The theme worked better than I had expected. I liked various things about the game, but the blind imp bidding was just really annoying. And it reminded me that I really prefer games of below 3 weight rather than above 3. I would give this about a 3.3.

After reading more, it sounds like the game is intended to be played more as a light chaotic romp, rather than as a deep thinker. The only way I can think of to do that would be to enforce a time limit for grouping your imps--maybe 30 seconds. And then perhaps another 30 seconds for choosing your cards. But I would absolutely hate it that way.

RATING: 7
I've really enjoyed all of Vlaada's games. Mage Knight and Galaxy Trucker are both in my top 10. So I was confident enough in this that I actually bought it without having played it. It a very good game, though not as great as the others I mention. Thematically, each player is controlling a group of imps that have started a pet shop to raise monsters, show them at exhibitions, and sell them to finicky dungeon lords. The action selection mechanism is interesting. Players secretly make 1-6 lots using imps and gold. Then all lots are revealed and players choose actions with each lot from largest to smallest. Action spaces allow players to purchase pets petz, cages, food, upgrades, etc., as well as doing various other things. After the actions are resolved, each player draws needs cards based on the petz he controls. Then the player must assign needs to his petz and meet those needs to avoid bad things happening. Because of the vagaries of the draw when receiving needs cards, it is sometimes impossible to meet all the needs of one's petz. At this point, it becomes a game of puzzle-solving in order to do the least harm. The needs cards also affect the phases in which petz are shown and sold, as the judges of the exhibition and the buyers of the petz have particular likes and dislikes. The needs cards played on a given pet affect how it is rated and how much a buyer will want it.

Like many of Vlaada's games, there is a bit of a steep barrier to entry as there are intricate rules for each phase of the game. However, I found that once we were up and running, the game play was very smooth - even more so than Dungeon Lords. The theme and art of Dungeon Petz go a long way to making this an enjoyable ride. If you enjoy Vlaada's games, I think you'll like this. If you find them to be tedious optimization puzzles, I don't think Dungeon Petz will change your mind.

RATING: 7

Overall, I like this game.  I think it is starting to show its age a bit...the worker placement genre is absolutely loaded these days...but for what it is, it is a very good example.

RATING: 2
Zooloretto with difficult to manage pets and hard to please customers. So perhaps it is closer to A Little Shop of Horrors. It becomes a difficult exercise of resource management, being blocked by your competitors and avoiding disasters.

Each turn is a puzzle to be solved. From the blind bidding for the turn order to the limited number of actions and resources, to matching your pets needs to the resources you have. This puzzle becomes more difficult as you acquire more pets. Not to mention the longer range problem of having the right kind of pet for the shows and customers. 

It is a gamer's game. Very challenging with a lot of moving parts and considerations. A little too chaotic for its forward planning aspects. Could see a huge issue with AP.

Had to downrate since it single handily almost turned my wife into a non-gamer.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
So far, the best worker placement game for me. You'll either love the theme and artwork or hate it, making it a poor game for you. If you like it, though, it's just supercool. German worker placement games are usually insanely dull, with all these buildings/roads built and generic resource gathered (money/stone/whatnot). Here, you have a chaotic and funny environment, meat and vegetables, adorable monsters, hidden betting with actual figurines, and a few minigames hidden inside the game, with the card "needs assignment" being the most cool one. It's one of those rare games where the theme is everything. It makes the game, it makes the gameplay. You will not "play the game", you will send goblins, build cages, care about animals and make a show off during a fair. The immersion is amazing.

Rulebook is quite funny, but written in such a way, that it's hard to follow it and first time, you'll get some troubles understanding at the rules. After that, the game does not drag anymore and it's just a lot of laugh. 

Yes, your petz will poop a lot. Yes, it's a great topic for making silly jokes in-game.

RATING: 6
There are a lot of things happening in this game and it felt just a bit too limiting for me to rate it higher.  What I mean is that while the game does take a bit of time to go through, you don't really have access to much while you are playing.  i.e. Only one player getting access to collect more workers per round.

The art is pleasant and the pets are fun.  There is a great deal of resource management happening here and the worker placement is definitely the driving mechanism, but also seems to fall to the background since that phase of the game goes so quickly and the majority of your time is spent in other phases.

While I would happily play this game again, it's not one that I'm going to be asking to bring to the table.  A bit too much time to setup and play for the fun I get out of it.

Great game, always willing to play this.

RATING: 7
This is the first Eurogame that I decided I really wanted, in about March 2012 - and also the first game by Vlaada Chvatil that I bought (he is one of my absolute favourite designers, along with Stefan Feld). It has everything that one would come to expect from Vlaada - mechanics which provide innovative twists on classic Eurogame mechanics, a brilliant theme which really shines through, a rulebook which is hilarious while remaining a joy to read and explaining the game very well, and gameplay which requires difficult decisions and can even be quite punishing if you are not careful.

This is also one of the few Vlaada games which I can get my game group to play, if not that often. But we haven't played this since at least 2013, and mainly due to my own preferences. This is now easily my least favourite Vlaada game that I own - I think it is much more luck-dependent that say Dungeon Lords, and the blind-bidding mechanic, while both original and fun, doesn't provide hair-raising decisions in the way DL's action-selection mechanic does. I still think this is a good game, but I now never find myself actively wanting to play it.

RATING: 8.2
(+) The theme is wonderful and fits the game experience perfectly!
(+) Unlike many heavily themed games, this is a clever and elegant euro-style game which offers many difficult and interesting choices
(+) There is a luck-element involved with drawing the need cards for your pets. I usually dislike luck-driven elements in a game, but here this really adds to the feel you are taking care of cute and unpredictable baby pets. Moreover, you can influence your chances and have different ways to cope with a bad card draw.
(+) A lot of game for a relatively simple set of rules. The rules are pretty intuitive, which makes it easy to teach the game.
(+) The game relies heavily on making efficient decisions and good planning.
(+) The worker placement part in which you have to assign groups is challenging and very tense, but forgiving. There is always something useful to do and you can assign the workers differently should other players surprise you with the groups they assign to actions on the game board

(-) I like the mechanism by which you draw the need cards for your pets and the fact that you have a limited amount of influence and insight as to what to expect, but still a bad card draw can be quite problematic in your overall strategy. I generally dislike luck-driven elements in games. Luckily I think the system with the need cards is really cute, so I'm a bit more forgiving for this mechanism in this game... There are also multiple ways to cope with a bad card draw.

Playtime: 35 - 40 min / player

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
 I am not particularly keen on worker placement games, but this one struck a chord. It possesses fairly whimsical yet poignant theme of raising monster pets, which I am keen on. But more importantly, I enjoy the hidden bid for turn order which prefaces every turn you play. This is what separates it from your everyday worker-placement game. It asks interesting questions with regard to value, and adds some tension to what would be a normally lackluster mechanism. No longer are you consistently relegated to turn order to select your place of action, you have some means to subvert others and advance on their turn. Interestingly enough, each time I play this game, it hums along almost seamlessly (after the initial rules hump), as many of the interlocking rules and exceptions are thematically connected.  

(+) Hidden bid for turn order adds tension. 
(+) Strong thematic connection of rules and exceptions make gameplay intuitive. 
(+) Rewards strategic planning and mitigation. 
 
(-) Element of randomness can give some real sour 'needs' draws.
(-) At high player counts, you may be at a loss of useful placements if you cannot/do not bid competitively. 
(-) May lack longevity, as the re-playability seems to lie in variability rather than underlying depth. The length hurts it from being something that regularly hits the table, so this may not be a big factor.

RATING: 8.5
You wouldn't think that a game this complex and clunky-looking would evoke strong imaginations of the theme, but the masterful Vlaada offers another amazing game experience here.  It may be silly and lighthearted in presentation, but this is a very tense game that can make you feel on the brink of a downward spiral each round, as you struggle to maintain your creatures while also doing enough to impress judges and customers enough to win the game.  Many times each game, you'll breathe a sigh of relief as you get to take the action you *needed* in order to avoid disaster, or panic as you look desperately for an alternative to the action you *thought* you needed that was just taken.  I was careful in specifying that the game merely looks clunky at a glance; this is more than sound mechanically, and presents its theme extremely well.

RATING: 9.2
Somewhat more accessible then Dungeon Lords and definitely not as punishing. There are still places where something can go horribly wrong, but it's easier to avoid them. However, if you play well and don't take too much risk, you are unlikely to lose the effects of your work or have them destroyed as in Dungeon Lords. I think it gives you more control, with fewer things going wrong by chance, while still giving a feeling of manoeuvring on the verge of a catastrophe. Tight resources, emotions from not knowing if you'll be able to take the action you need, deep and fun theme. I wish there where more games like it.

"OK, I can't let my monster get too bored or hungry, so I can play these cards here... but I will lose points if I let him mutate because of this card... AND the poop!  I have to clean the poop!"
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
We first played Dungeon Lords this year, which I ended up liking quite a bit. Then someone was selling a pre-owned copy of Dungeon Petz, so I added it to my shelves. It's another good game.

The designer definitely built this around the main concept of the story it is trying to tell without first focusing on specific game elements. The concept of starting a pet shop and managing live animals for a while and showing them off before selling them. You have to have cages for your animals, and the pets have different needs, such as hunger and playfulness and pooping. Can your cage contain any tantrums they might have? Or will magical outbursts from the pets be too much for the cage to contain?

But some interesting game play elements are strong enough to make this a good game, too. You put your workers in groups, and the largest groups get first choice of the action spots. Also, you have a bit of control over the needs that you assign to the pets which, combined with knowledge about future scoring rounds, allows you to plan ahead about how best to play.

It's a unique game. And the experience is somewhat whimsical. I don't really like some of the upkeep that the game requires. (Did I put out the new cages? Oh, I forgot to refill the food stands.) But it is fun to try to puzzle out which pets should have certain needs and how to manage your worker imps on what to do. And all the poop talk makes us laugh.

RATING: 7.6
Provisional rating: only played once all the way through (2 player) and solo-ed a few times.

I appreciate how the distinct parts of the game- shopping, pet care, and selling/showing pets- are carefully united through the inter-functioning of the game mechanisms (worker placement gets you better stuff for matching the needs symbols; effectively matched needs yield better scoring, etc) and through the theme of running a pet shop.

Beautiful to look at with wonderful little plastic goblins pieces. Seriously funny rulebook. Cute Petz.

I just wish the rules weren't so involved, so that the barrier for entry would be lower.

RATING: 6
Loads of rules, but I don't mind because 1) they aren't ambiguous (looking at you M&M), and 2) they are all thematically motivated within the manual itself. Speaking of theme, this is the most thematic game I own; it's really amazing. 

The game is kind of fiddly, but it doesn't bother me. For some reason, my wife's AP doesn't manifest as strongly in this game as it does in others. Component quality is subpar (imo). 

Update: Starting to feel a bit samey with two, since the dummy imps follow a fixed pattern. This isn't a problem on the pet/cage/addon track, since all of these come out randomly. However, availability of the other action spaces follows a very specific pattern every game. This is most noticeable for food, relatives, and the auction space. My play has fallen into a particular rhythm, driven by the availability of these spots. As a result, I can't really recommend this for two anymore.

I guess another thing I don't care for is that it feels more like competitive puzzle solving than playing a game against each other.

RATING: 4.2
I've never before seen certain people from my gaming group suffer like during that one game of Dungeon Petz we played. It's fiddly, the hidden group setup phase makes it really hard to guess how much effort you need to spend on your biggest group to get the action you want, the assigning of needs to pets cries for micro-optimization, it's really hard to predict what you will draw and what you need to prepare for -- do you need to get food because you will draw lots of "hunger" cards, or should you get a cage upgrade to deal with all those "rage" cards? -- and I guess I could go on. It's cute, I grant it that much.

RATING: 8
Surprisingly thinky given the theme. This one has everything you come to expect from a Vlaada game. Not as complex as some of his other creations (Through the ages; Mage Knight), but an excellent game nonetheless.

What's particularly enjoyable (and sometimes frustrating!) is the unpredictable nature of the creatures themselves - we've had games where someone's stareplant went beserck and went rampaging across the countryside, or some cute little monster mutated and ultimately blinked out of existence.

You can make contingency plans in Dungeon Petz, but you will never have perfect control of your 'pets' so if that kind of randomness irritates you, it may not be the right game for you.

RATING: 7
[2012-05-12]
A Dungeon Lords történetét továbbszőtte a szerző és egy új játékot alkotott, amiben impjeink vezető nélkül maradnak és vállalkozásba kezdenek. Szörnytenyésztő szerepébe bújnak. Szörnyeket szerzünk koboldjainkkal a négy lehetséges ketrecünkbe, versenyeztetjük őket, felneveljük, majd minél jobb áron el próbáljuk adni. Gyönyörű kivitel, vicces megoldások. A játék sima munkáslerakós. Nekem az akciósorrend végrehajtása volt új. Attól függően, hogy mennyi emberünket szánjuk sorban az egyes akcióinkra annál korábban jöhetünk. Érdekesek az állatok mindig változó kártyás megoldással megvalósított igényei. Ami nem tetszett, hogy az állatokat megvevő kereskedők csak egy kőrrel korábban derülnek ki az alapjátékban így nagy a szerencsefaktor az egyes állatok eladási árait tekintve. Egy szerencsésen beszerezett állat az egész partit eldöntheti. A fix 5 kör teljesen rendben van. A játékidő elsőre 3 óra volt, de szerintem olyan 2 körülire 4 fővel is lenyomható másodikra. Sajnos a szabálymagyarázat kb. 1 órás volt. Nehéz játék a Dungeon Petz, talán még az elődjénél is nehezebb, nagyon sok kis szabály van. Igazi gémer játék. Nekem a Lords az egyedisége miatt jobban tetszik, de teljesen rendben levő játék. Még nőhet a pontszáma.

RATING: 9

Well, this one is another winner for sure. Taking care of your cutesy pets is REALLY challenging though - please dont let the artwork fool you into thinking that this is a lite game - NO WAY. Very challenging, even sometimes stressful, but a very rewarding experience.

RATING: 7
1 play, 4er

I love the art, but unfortunately not the game. It's a good game that requires a lot of planning, has its share of tension from the simultaneous semi-blind (but in a good way) bidding followed by worker allocation for limited locations/actions. Actually, the game works really well! And the monster tokens (dials which show a lot of information) are very nicely done! I just didn't quite as much fun with it as I'd hoped ... will have to try it again.

--- Summary ---
Players each have:
- a board with space for 4 cages (1 preprinted), each of which may have 1 improvement. Cages, used to hold animals, have traits (strength, magic, play, poo-absorption) with values summing to 3.
- a play mat, with spaces to store:
... food (meat or veggies; tracks show how they spoil in 2/3 turns)
... imps (start with 6)
... artifacts (collected during game; each give different power)
... gold
- 4 cards, 1/color; these show one of several types of icons (food, magic, strength, play, poo, disease) with each color deck having a different distribution of these icons.

The board has several areas, each with one or more worker-spots to place imps (total 14 spots available, some with specific minimum requirements eg 2+ imps or imps AND 1+ coin). These are:
- Buy cage (max 4/player)
- Buy cage improvement (max 1/cage)
- Buy baby monster (each round, seed with 3)
- Buy older monster (start game with 1; at end of each round, any here die and you move any remaining babies up to this area)
- Market platform (when you sell, get 3x price instead of usual 2x)
- Get more imps (start game with 4 imps on the board; each turn, one more becomes available for recruitment)
- Judge exhibit (get +2 modifier when showing animal)
Also, the board shows the requirements for upcoming competitions and customers (specific qualities are in demand each turn, determined by random tiles and revealed 2 turns in advance).

Each turn, collect income (1 or 2, based on turn order). Then simultaneously allocate imps/coins into groups; then reveal. Players allocate groups in descending order of size, ties broken by turn order. Take action immediately when placing imps. Then, draw additional cards based on animal attributes; assign these to each monster, then resolve: need to satisfy all card demands (based on cages, stored food, etc.) or suffer penalties (get tokens modifying monster value; possibly kill monster, etc.). Next, players may exhibit 1+ animals (as determined by exhibit tile; early turns skip this), earning exhibit points; earn VP based on _rank_ of exhibit points. Then, players may sell animals, max 1/player/customer -- earn VP based on demands shown on customer tile, and price based on monster (+ price modifiers on customer tile).

At the end of your turn, any unused imps can be used to earn gold or clean poo out of empty cages.

The game ends after 5 rounds, and then you do a final scoring. Most points wins!

RATING: 7
There's a lot to juggle in this game and that makes it not at all for everybody.  It is incredibly deep and complicated for a game with such a cutesy theme, but depth and complication seem to come hand-in-hand with Mr. Chvatil.

First off, the theme is very cute and fun and the artwork on all the boards and cards is gorgeous (although a bit too busy for my tastes).  The quality of the components is passable but the boards could use to be a little thicker, they will wear out after a number of games from constant folding.  And the imp figures are dangerously sharp!

The actual gameplay is original and elegant, something I've also come to expect from Chvatil.  The play order mechanic of divvying up your imps and gold is great and offers a mini-game all its own where you have to weigh the importance of first pick over more picks over leaving imps behind for other actions, etc.  The spaces offer a variety of actions that each are valuable although I miss the variety and variability of other games, there are never new spaces added to the game.

This one is a brain-burner despite the fact that the small number of action spaces doesn't change and neither do your pets needs and all of this information is available well in advance (you always know a turn ahead what you need for an exhibition, 2 turns ahead what you need to sell to customers, and for your entire pet's life what needs you will need to satisfy as it grows).  However, even though all this information is open there is certainly a lot of it and balancing how to satisfy all your pets needs, win exhibitions, and make your customers happy can be quite the mental workout.

I definitely need to play this game with the full four, I wasn't fond of the arbitrary cut off from certain spaces in the 2- and 3-player game.  I got rid of my copy though as it was just collecting dust in the shadow of so many better worker placement games on my shelf.

RATING: 8
Worker placement games have slowly made it to the top of my favorite mechanics list. If you now pair that with the best theme ever (which I definitely granted bonus points for): running a monster pet store for Dungeonlords; and an adorably cute art style you made a winner. At least for me, right up my street. I really love how it is thematically thought through (eg. your food goes bad if you store it too long, the pets poop into their cages. It is a game with poop cubes!!!).
Yes, the scoring seemed a bit fiddly to me (maybe that was because it was my first time playing) and the downtime when everyone is picking their cards to attend to the petz's needs has a chance to be annoying, but I really enjoyed my time with Dungeon Petz and happily play more of it.

RATING: 7
Lovely and humorous theme. This game really sets the golden standard for how thematic a euro can be. And by "thematic" I don't just mean that it has a theme, I mean that the rules and mechanisms tie into that theme.

It's a complicated game, but the rules are surprisingly easy to learn (relative to how heavy it is), because it really helps that every single rule has a very good thematic explanation.

While there is only indirect player interaction, like in most worker placement games, the level of conflict is actually really quite high, and the game makes it easy to royally screw you opponent over, if you like. In fact, you tend to royally screw your opponent over even if you actively try not to. Most worker placements give you several alternatives. If the good resource spot is taken, use the one that's not as good. Or get more of another resource and trade for the one you need. That kind of thing. In Dungeon Petz, you will very often find yourself in the situation that there is one slot, and one slot only, that will get you what you need, and if you don't get it, bad things will happen. So planning ahead becomes extremely important, even for a worker placement game.

The Dark Alleys expansion does alleviate this a bit though.

RATING: 7
Felt a bit short, but at the same time good length, since anything more would just be straight repetition.  Tons of symbology which makes the teach a bit of a struggle.  But the bidding of workers was a really cool mechanism, and there were a lot of really good things in the game.  Aging of Petz was cool, the playing of the cards to fulfill needs was cool. Being only able to carry over 1 card of each color specifically limits options for planning.  And lastly, the scoring mechanism I feel makes the game artificially close, since you don't get points for having attributes, but rather points that turn into other points,  Overall solid medium weight game that I would play if someone wanted to.

RATING: 7.8

I like the worker placement spots, I particularly love assigning the cards to your monsters, and working out how you will try and score them. If you like euros and you want to try something different, I recommend Dungeon Petz.

RATING: 7
:thumbsup: Flawless theme-mechanisms integration. The game was build around the theme, with painstaking attention to detail, and not a single thing is out of place here.
:thumbsup: How adorable is it to raise baby dungeon pets?!
:thumbsup: Underneath is a solid worker placement euro game with an interesting blind bidding twist, seamlessly integrated with a unique pet store management card game.
:thumbsup: The game offers a moral dilemma: do you focus all your attention on raising 1 or 2 pets and making sure they get the best care possible, or do you go for 3-4 pets and risk overlooking something and neglecting them? The game punishes you for neglecting your pets (you won't get a good review from selling sad pets), but both approaches done skillfully can lead to victory.
:thumbsup: Every game there will be a different combination of customers, competitions, pets, and artifacts, which will encourage you to think on the spot and tailor your strategy accordingly.
:thumbsup: Raising the right pet for the right customer and making sure it looks its best on the day is intellectually engaging and full of tough choices.
:thumbsup: In the tradition of Vlaada's other thematic masterpieces, the rulebook really helps in immersing you into the theme and thematically justifying the game mechanisms.
:thumbsup: All art in the game, including the board and player screens, is phenomenal. It lets the theme shine and enhances the gameplay a lot.
[b][color=green]------------------------------[/color][/b]
:!block: The game is heavy and difficult for its lighthearted appearance. But you could tone it down by only focusing on 1 pet at a time as your strategy, which makes it significantly easier to manage.
[b][color=red]------------------------------[/color][/b]
:thumbsdown: The worker placement phase may create a lot of analysis paralysis. You are required to plan your entire turn ahead, arrange your workers in groups of bet sizes, figure out what spots you need, predict how your opponent is going to arrange their workers, and what spots your opponent needs. All that needs to be done in your head before actually placing any workers. And coupled with the fact that managing the needs of multiple pets can get quite complex on its own, the planning phase can get very difficult.
:thumbsdown: With 2 players the dummy workers do their job in tightening up the board, do not require you to manage them so you can focus on your own game, but we still don't like them. They put the game on rails, to an extent. In every game dummy workers will block the exact same spots at any given round, and this skews the gameplay towards certain powerful actions at certain times, which are the same every game. There's still variability based on the order of the pets, clients, and competitions, but we can't help but think how much more freedom the game will have with 4 players, where every action space is available every round—if you can get there first that is.

This game really is a hardcore micromanagement game.Well, actually I have to disagree with me here a bit.As with all worker placement games it's very much on the tactical scale as you always have to adapt to the choices left to you and you can never predict as you can't predict your coplayers' actions (at least not for sure unless you're telepathic) Nevertheless this a resource management game, and a very tight one at that. RATING: 4
An interesting take on worker placement. A nice mechanic where you have to secretly divide up your resources (imps and coins) and then you get to take actions based on who has the largest groups. A classic "go all in to be guaranteed to get the thing I need, or spread out to get more, possibly inferior, actions" scenario.

That all seems fine. The problem is with the amount of fiddly rules. Oh boy do I hate fiddly, edge-case rules, and this game is full of them. There are also multiple upkeep phases where you refill all of the areas of the board, yet again each one has its own fiddly rules.

Also the game doesn't work well with two players. It institutes multiple dummy workers on the board that add even more fiddliness (the workers move around and block, or even just partially block, different spaces).

I'm still giving this a 4 (as opposed to a lower rating) because the core game seems like it could be really good. It's just that all of the extra complexity and special rules bring it down. I wanted to like it, but it's just way more work than fun.

Sold.

RATING: 8
Dungeon Pets (I refuse to spell it incorrectly, and I'm utterly disappointed that the designers/publishers decided to use the "z") is an interesting affair. On one hand, you have a very cute, cuddly, nice looking game. Children and adults alike fall in love with it and the idea of its theme almost instantly. But...many are deceived. Once opened and discovered, the mechanics combine to be a grueling exercise in disaster management and crowded board space with you opponents. The pets are hard to manage, the customers are tough to please, and the opposing players inevitably get in the way. Children will struggle. It is a challenging game, with plenty of rules to learn. It's a gamer's game entirely. But I feel that the theme is so richly integrated into the mechanics, that it's very manageable to teach and learn. Vlaada Chvatil is one of my most admired designers. This game keeps him admirable.
[COLOR=#FFFFFF]TAGS: thematic, euro, Vlaada Chvatil, worker placement, bidding, auctioning, simultaneous action selection, miniatures, artwork, individual player boards, complex[/COLOR]

RATING: 4
Huge disappointment for me. :( I had heard so many good things, and I was really hoping this was the one Vlaada game I'd actually enjoy.

The theme is great - if you ignore the fact that you're essentially abusing animals for profit...wait, don't ignore that, that's terrible!

What I meant to say, is that the theme goes well with the mechanics. And the artwork is AWESOME. But the game is just a big fiddly mess. Too many finicky rules to keep track of. The "needs" phase where everyone is locking in cards is just annoying and painfully slow. And the game is swingy as hell. Also, there are so few action spaces that you can get completely screwed out of doing what you need to do, especially if someone else is flush with gold.

I have come to the conclusion that I just don't like Vlaada's games, and I can't fathom why other people do.

RATING: 5.5
First Impression:

A highly thematic euro plentiful in humor and great art. Definately a "one of a kind" game. (or in this even more special case "two of a kind").

I don't like fantasy themes but I love theme integration and this game is an example to the whole market in this regard. You even manage poo in this game in a fun way! In addition there are several interesting mechanics, and little manageble luck which overall make for an interesting game beneath the layers of theme.

However, the game is too fiddly for me, and the amount of time wasted in flipping, drawing, replenishing and all the little things combined easily make a 90mins experience drag for 3 hours. That makes the game outstay its welcome. In addition there are tons of minor rules (typical Vlaada I guess) that are occassionally frustrating, although I'm ok with those that add to the theme.

All in all, I have strong feelings about this game, as I both loved it and hated it for different reasons. I'd like to play more, to see which feeling persists, and which fades away.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
There are those who think that Vlaada Chvatil is a genius. For the most part, I'm not one of them. I find Mage Knight tedious. I think Galaxy Trucker works more as a puzzle than a game. Space Alert is hilarious, but again I'm not convinced it's a good game. I do enjoy Through the Ages, despite the fact that it's the type of game I usually don't, but that's mostly because I play it online... where the time investment is broken up into asynchronous moves over a week or so. And I didn't really care for Dungeon Lords, the game Dungeon Petz is nominally descended from. 

But I really love Dungeon Petz. I love the theme of raising a monster pet. And everything in the game makes thematic sense. The turn order mechanism is brilliant, you allocate your workers into groups and bigger groups go first, but a group can only do one action. And the way you fill the needs of your pets is pretty sharp too. Each pet has a series of colored needs which will increase as they age. You draw a card of the corresponding color for each color on your pets and allocate them to each of your pets. While cards usually mean you need one type of thing, they occasionally mean something else. So while green needs are usually food, occasionally it might be that your pet needs to play. Then you scramble to figure out how best to allocate your cards to your pets so that you can actually satisfy their needs adequately. Additionally, every turn there will be a competition judging your pets on some random metric and a customer will appear wanting a pet with specific needs. But you'll only know a turn or so beforehand what the competition and customer will be. So not only are you trying to meet all your pets needs, you're trying to assign needs in a way that will win competitions and please customers.

Edit: Downgraded rating slightly because the game is a little bit too dependent on luck.

RATING: 7
Lots of cool sub-systems in this Vlaada title. Balancing pets' needs with the exhibitions and customers takes some finesse. Buying the baby pets with needs that match your cages (and getting cages/addons that will fit your current menagerie) is tricky to get down. The specifics of imp grouping and placement are a nice interactive element, in classic Vlaada "guess your opponents' intentions" fashion. There are a lot of little thematic rules everywhere, but to me at least, the player aid does a great job at pinning those down.

This will probably see about as much play as Dungeon Lords. Maybe a little more, since I may be more willing to teach this to new players.

RATING: 10
Woah! This game is everything I hoped for and more! First off, it is full of great decisions. Theses decisions may include blocking an opponent, setting yourself up for future rounds, and debating on whether or not to press your luck for big reward - risking the life of your adorable pet for the sake of your stores reputation. Furthermore, the theme is so strong that it's become both me and my 8 year old sisters favorite game! It's strategic enough for gamers and the theme allows for non-gamers to simply have fun as they try to run their own store. My only complaint thus far has been the amount of time it takes to teach the rules. The theme and both humorous and well-written rule book  makes teaching the game easier, however expect to spend 15-30 minutes both setting up and teaching the game before starting. And that shouldn't be until after you've studied the rules and solo-tested the game yourself!

RATING: 6.5
Still a pretty early impression.  I adore the theme and artwork for this game, it's a delight to look at.  It creates stories, which I love.  It's Worker Placement but with a lot of fresh twists through a sort of blind bid for placement and luck mitigation throughout, another thing I love.

The downside though is that it's pretty non-interactive aside from a bit of competition for spots.  It's pretty clear who is going to win most events, though maybe that's a negative towards the 2-player game.

I like it quite a bit, but it hasn't grabbed me as much as I was expecting.  I worry that there's not enough variability or paths to victory to really keep my interest long term.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Budget a ridiculous amount of time to learn the game first time around. Somebody _really_ needs to have read the rules several times before you start if you want your first play to be streamlined. There are a LOT of rules.

Because you're only ever looking 2 turns ahead and your choices are constrained by others' actions, the decision space isn't too brain burny once you're in to it. A nice balance between tactical and short-term strategic- more tactical than Terra Firma, for example.

I enjoyed the manic sense of barely contained chaos, and being forced to make painful and hilarious trade offs. There's luck, but enough information on the table that you can understand and optimise the risks you take.

Sacrifices elegance for theme, which I don't mind because all the rules _do_ make sense, thematically. 

I'd give it a higher rating if I had people to play it with regularly. You need a couple of plays to be comfortable enough to properly enjoy it. As it is, you need 3-4 hours for the first play with new people which makes it hard to get out. 

BBG's rating system needs some way to rate games based on context- if you've got a bunch of experienced gamers who want a bit of a thematic muck about then this is perfect.

Rather than live off the system, those unemployed imps have moved to the surface and started their very own pet store. Dungeon Lords have to get those critters that haunt their dungeon somewhere, right?

Dungeon Petz is a vast improvement over its parent game. I love Dungeon Lords and I still think it is a very good game, but it is a very challenging to teach. While there is still quite a bit to learn in Dungeon Petz, it is much easier to just dive into it and cover the rules as they come up.

RATING: 6
[i]Rating after one 4p game:[/i]

Although there are worker placement games out there I enjoy  much more (because at it's core, Dungeon Petz is just a worker placement/hand management game), I gotta say that this is a neat, clever design - the theme and artwork are just lovely and perfectly tied to the mechanics. Theme-wise it's a breath of fresh air among all the "typical" euro games. The major drawback is that drawing the need cards can be too random at times. You can plan your round perfectly only to get all the cards you don't want. 

All in all - it's a pretty enjoyable experience, but nothing more. There's just something about this game that prevents me from liking it more, but I can't put my finger on it.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.8
We’ve played Dungeon Lords about 1½ year ago. I really liked the mechanisms the game uses, but the theme…. I was way too dark for me and M. When I heard that in 2011 Dungeon Petz was coming on the market, I wasn’t very interested in it. Just because of the theme of DL. When I saw the first artwork of DP, suddenly I became very interested. It was much ‘lighter’ then DL is, and somehow, it seemed more funny to me. Next thing I noticed about this game, was the pricing. Ouch…

It really has to be a very good game, and there has to be a fantastic quality of game pieces inside the box, to justify the price of €55/$75. Luckily for us, we got the opportunity to rent it at the GGC, so we would be able to try this game before we decide whether or not to buy this one.

Easy said, easy done. Picked the game up last month, and first thing I did, was set up a 2-player game, to get an idea of the game. It was a lot of work to set it up, because the amount of components inside the game box is huge. The, very nice made, imps, known from the game of DL, are also in the box. I read the rules, and believed it wasn’t a very hard game to play. But anyhow, I decided, that I would be handy to have a reference card which shows the turn order in each phase of a round. There was no Dutch one, so I had to produce on for myself. I used the artwork of the rules book to make a nice, at least I think it’s nice, reference card (click on it, to see a bigger version of it).

Finally, today was the day DP was set up again. It took about 25 minutes to set up a 2-player game, then it was time to explain the game to M. As I didn’t re-read the rules, and the first time I’ve read them, I just did it briefly, it took aboy 2 hours, before I explained the game and all of its options to M. It isn’t that hard to do, but there are a lot of possibilities, and little, pretty unique, mechanics in the game. Once explained, we started to play, and the reference card I made, turned out to be really handy. We played it instantly, and it was very clear what we had to do. We only had to grab the rule book once or twice to look up for the meaning of a special token, that showed up during the game. Basically, there are only three area’s on the main board, where you can set your imps to work. That is the market, where you can buy food or special tokens. The, what I call, the Zoo, where you can buy your pets and/or cages and the (also my own name), administration area, where you can send your imps to the hospital, or sell your pets for a higher value, or can give yourself some bonus points at the exhibition. Really, in basic that’s all there is. 3 main area’ and in each area, you can choose about 3 different kind of actions. So that’s makes a total of 9 possible actions. That shouldn’t be difficult to understand.

But, choosing actions is only step 2b in your phase of 6. Each step is divided in smaller steps, so really, there is a lot of possibilities. Most of the steps are really small to do. For example step 5 in a phase is divided in 3 smaller steps. Step a is to sell 1 pet. Step b, is to discard your cards and step c is earn money and/or clean your cages. Step a, might take some time, because you have to look and calculate what is the best pet to sell this round, while step b is taking you about 10 seconds, and step c could be resolved in less then a minute. First time we played this game, it took us about 90 minutes. And I think, when you play this with 2, it could be done is something more than 1 hour.

After playing it’s time for a verdict. Do I like this game? Yes, I do. I like it more then I liked DL. Mainly because of the difference in the theme. Gameplay is very nice. All there is left, is the pretty huge price of the game. Is it justified? When I use the next formula I could easily reach a conclusion: (Components + Gameplay (mechanics) + Fun) x 2 = Price.

Components : 7½
Gameplay: 8
Fun : 7¾
(7½+8+7¾) = 23¼ x 2 = 46½
I’ll be short 8½ on this one, so is the price justified? When using this formula, no it is not. But you can always add a component to the formula. A component called something like ‘need’ or ‘want’. There is always a way to make the price justified. For us? I wait until the price will drop something.

RATING: 7
*Expansions/Promos Own - Dark Alleys,Bonus Petz, and Bob the Blob.

RATING: 8.5
Chvatil never disappoints. This game is very well designed. I've never seen a mechanic like this one before (not to say its not out there). While Dungeon Petz is not as brutal as its cousin Dungeon Lords it still brings a satisfyingly tough puzzle-strategy game to the table. The large majority of my experience with this game is in the two player setting. My wife and I love this game and it really plays well with two players. If you are looking for a game similar to Dungeon Lords that isn't as difficult this is it.
